First objection: God's foreknowledge negates lordship and servitude
Objection against divine foreknowledge of particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against God's foreknowledge of particulars and its two arguments concerning lordship and servitude.

Core Idea
If God knows everything in advance, nothing could be otherwise — so where is divine power and human choice?

This objection challenges the compatibility of divine foreknowledge with both God's lordship and human servitude. The argument claims that if God knows all particulars before they happen, those events become necessary, stripping God of power and humans of free will. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'لما' and 'فاء' in the Arabic.
2
Identify the two main claims
The objector makes two separate but related claims: one about lordship (rubūbiyya) and one about servitude (ʿubūdiyya). Find how each is argued.
3
Reflect on the experiential argument
The objector adds an experiential proof: we feel we have power to act or refrain. Ask yourself why this is presented as a separate argument.
This argument is meant to show the absurdity of denying human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : لو كان الله تعالى عالماً بهذه الجزئيات قبل وقوعها لبطلت الربوبية ولبطلت العبودية . أما بطلان الربوبية ، فلأن الذي علم وقوعه يكون واجب الوقوع . وما كان واجب الوقوع امتنع كون القادر قادرا عليه ، فوجب أن لا يقدر الله على شيء . وذلك يقدح في الربوبية وأما بطلان العبودية فلان غير ما ذكرناه يقتضي أن لا يقدر العبد على شيء البتة ، وذلك يمنع من كونه قادراً على العبودية. وأيضاً : فإنا نجد بالضرورة من أنفسنا أنا إن شئنا الفعل قدرنا عليه ، وإن شئنا الترك قدرنا عليه ، فالقول بأنه لا قدرة لنا على الفعل والترك مكابرة في المحسوسات والقول بأنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات قبل دخولها في الوجود يمنع من تلك القدرة ، فكان القول به باطلاً .
The first argument: They said: If God Almighty knew these particulars before their occurrence, then lordship (rubūbiyya) would be invalid and servitude (ʿubūdiyya) would be invalid. As for the invalidation of lordship, because that whose occurrence is known is necessarily occurring. And what is necessarily occurring prevents the omnipotent from being powerful over it, so it must be that God has no power over anything. That impugns lordship. As for the invalidation of servitude, because besides what we mentioned, it entails that the servant has no power over anything at all, and that prevents him from being capable of servitude. Also, we find necessarily in ourselves that if we will an act we are able to do it, and if we will to refrain we are able to do it. So the claim that we have no power over act and refrain is obstinacy against sensory perceptions. And the claim that He Almighty is knower of particulars before their entry into existence prevents that power, so the claim is false.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against God's knowledge of particulars before they occur. The objector argues that such foreknowledge invalidates both divine lordship (rubūbiyya) and human servitude (ʿubūdiyya). For lordship: if God knows an event will occur, it becomes necessary, and God can no longer have power to change it, thus undermining His omnipotence and lordship. For servitude: the same line of reasoning implies that humans have no real power over their actions, so they cannot be held accountable for servitude. Moreover, we have immediate experience of our own power: when we will to act, we act; when we will to refrain, we refrain. Denying this power is obstinacy. Since foreknowledge would negate this experienced power, the objector concludes that the claim of foreknowledge is false.

Key Terms
الربوبية
العبودية
قدرة
علم
جزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that this objection denies divine knowledge altogether, but it only denies knowledge that implies necessity.
Correction: The objector does not deny that God knows all things eternally; rather, they argue that if knowledge itself makes future events necessary, then both divine power and human free will are negated. The objection targets the modal implication of foreknowledge, not the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector say that God's foreknowledge would invalidate lordship?
A) Because God would not know what He is doing.
B) Because what is known to occur becomes necessary, so God would not be powerful over it.
C) Because lordship requires ignorance.
D) Because God would be dependent on creation.

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that divine foreknowledge of particulars eliminates both divine power over those events and human power over actions, based on necessity and direct experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though God's knowledge of your every action frees you to choose obedience, or do you secretly use it as an excuse to resign yourself to sin? Which belief leads you to repent more sincerely—that God knew your sin before you committed it, or that He gave you the power to choose otherwise?
?
When you fail to do what is right, do you blame God's decree or your own will? If you honestly examined your heart, would you find that you use the doctrine of divine knowledge to evade personal responsibility?
?
If you believed that God's foreknowledge did not make your actions necessary, would that change how you approach your next moment of temptation? How can you actively affirm your own God-given power to choose, rather than passively accepting that 'everything is already written'?

The Second Objection: Pure Non-Existence Cannot Be Known
The objector argues against knowledge of non-existents using the nature of pure non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the knowability of non-existent particulars and the objector's response to a potential counterargument.

Core Idea
Pure non-existence lacks the distinction that knowledge requires.

This lesson presents the second objection against the claim that God knows non-existent things. The objector argues that before existence, a thing is pure non-existence, which cannot possess any distinguishing attributes. Since knowledge requires the known to be distinct, non-existents cannot be known. The objector also anticipates a counter and shows its inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation to understand the key points.
Notice the structure: premise, conclusion, anticipated counter, rebuttal.
2
Identify the key premise
The objector claims that pure non-existence cannot have distinction (imtiyāz) or specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) by attributes. This is the foundation of the argument.
Distinction is necessary for anything to be known.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Since known things must be distinct, and non-existents lack distinction, the objector concludes that non-existents cannot be known.
This directly challenges the claim that God knows non-existent particulars.
4
Examine the potential counter and rebuttal
The objector anticipates someone saying that essences are established (mutāqarrara) even in non-existence. He rejects this, arguing that even those who say non-existents are things do not attribute composition, shape, color, etc. to them.
The rebuttal shows that the counter does not solve the problem; it only creates inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection matters for the broader discussion of divine attributes. How does it challenge the view that God knows all particulars before their existence?
This objection forces defenders to clarify the nature of God's knowledge and the status of non-existent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قالوا الشيء قبل دخوله في الوجود عدم محض ونفي صرف ، والعدم المحض يمتنع أن يحصل فيه الامتياز والاختصاص ( بالصفـة والخاصية ، وكل معلوم فإنه لا بد وأن يكون متميزاً عن غيره ، وأن يكون موصوفاً بصفات ) (١) وخواص لأجلها يحصل ذلك الامتياز . وهذا القياس ينتج أن المعدوم يمتنع كونه معلوماً ، ولا يقال : إنا إذا قلنا : الماهيات متقررة حال العدم . ، فقد زال هذا السؤال . لأنا نقول : هذا لا يفيد المطلوب . وذلك لأن القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء ، لا يقولون : إن تلك الذوات المعدومة تكون مؤلفة مركبة موصوفة بالشكل واللون والحصول في الحيز (٣) ، فيلزمكم أن تقولوا : إنه تعالى لا يعلم كون الذوات موصوفة بهذه الصفات ، إلا عند دخولها في الوجود ، وأنتم لا تقولون به ، وإذا جاز أن تقولوا : إنه تعالى يعلم كون هذه الذوات موصوفة بهذه الصفات . مع أن هذا غير حاصل في العلم ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال أيضاً : إنه تعالى يعلم بالماهيات قبل حصولها . مع أنها لا تكون حاصلة في العدم ؟
The second argument: They said: A thing before its entry into existence is pure non-existence and absolute negation. Pure non-existence cannot have distinction and specification by attribute and property. Every known thing must be distinct from others and be described by attributes and properties by which that distinction occurs. This reasoning yields that the non-existent cannot be known. And do not say: If we say that essences are established in the state of non-existence, then this question is removed. For we say: This does not achieve the desired. That is because those who say that the non-existent is a thing do not say that those non-existent entities are composed, composite, described by shape, color, and location in space. Then you must say that He Almighty does not know the entities as described by these attributes except at their entry into existence, and you do not say that. And if it is permissible for you to say that He Almighty knows these entities as described by these attributes while this is not present in knowledge, then why is it not permissible to also say that He Almighty knows the essences before their occurrence, even though they are not present in non-existence?
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against knowledge of non-existent particulars. The objector argues that before existence, a thing is pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ), which cannot have any distinguishing characteristics. Knowledge requires the known to be distinct and have attributes. Therefore, the non-existent cannot be known. The objector then anticipates a counterargument: some might say that essences are established (mutāqarrara) even in non-existence. The objector rejects this: even those who say non-existents are something do not attribute composition, shape, color, etc. to them in non-existence. If the opponent (the author) claims that God knows these entities with such attributes only when they exist, then he would be admitting that God does not know them before existence, which contradicts his own view. If the opponent says God knows them with attributes even before existence, then the objector says: If you allow that God knows these attributes while they are not present in knowledge (i.e., as known objects), then why not allow that God knows essences before they occur, even though they are not present in non-existence? This is a rebuttal, but it appears as part of the objection's reasoning to show inconsistency.

Key Terms
عدم محض
امتياز
اختصاص
صفات
ماهيات
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if non-existents are considered 'things', they must have attributes like shape, color, etc.
Correction: The objector clarifies that even those who claim non-existents are things do not attribute such properties to them; they are not composed or located in space.

Checkpoint
Why does the second objector claim that a non-existent thing cannot be known?
A) Because non-existence is a kind of existence.
B) Because pure non-existence lacks the distinction and attributes necessary for knowledge.
C) Because knowledge is only of physical objects.
D) Because God's knowledge is limited to existing things.

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues that pure non-existence cannot be known because it lacks the distinction required for any known object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate things that have not yet happened—your future, your plans, your hopes—do you treat them as 'pure non-existence' or as something with known attributes? How does this passage expose the difference between genuine knowledge and mere assumption?
?
The objector insists that every known thing must be distinct and described by attributes. In your own life, do you ever claim to 'know' God’s attributes or the unseen in a way that lacks real distinction in your mind? What would it take to sharpen that knowledge?
?
If pure non-existence cannot be known, what does that say about the time you spend worrying or imagining specific outcomes that may never come? Are you attending to what is truly present and knowable, or are you occupied with a vague nothingness?

Third Objection: Knowledge as Relation Implies a Vicious Circle
Objector argues knowledge as relation leads to circularity for non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining knowledge as a relation leads to the objection that God cannot know non-existent particulars without circularity.

Core Idea
If knowledge is a relation, then knowing the non-existent leads to a vicious circle.

The objector raises a logical difficulty: if knowledge is merely a relation between knower and known, then knowing something that does not yet exist seems impossible. This is because a relation requires both relata to be established, but a non-existent thing only comes into existence through God's power — which itself depends on His knowledge. Let's examine this objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of knowledge
The objector defines knowledge as a specific relation (nīsba) and addition (iḍāfa) between knower and known. Note that this definition is taken as established.
The objection does not argue about the definition but uses it as a premise.
2
Why a relation requires both terms
For a relation to occur, both relata must be 'established' (muqarrar) in themselves. This means the known must have some reality before it can be known.
Establishment does not necessarily mean existence, but a kind of fixity.
3
Apply to non-existent known
If the known is non-existent, it only becomes existent through God's power. But God's power to bring it into existence is conditioned by His knowledge of it. This creates a circle: knowledge requires existence of the known (via relation requirement), but existence requires knowledge.
The conclusion is that such a circle is impossible (muḥāl), so knowledge cannot be a relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن نقول : قد بينا أنه لا معنى للعلم إلا أنه نسبة مخصوصة وإضافة مخصوصة تحصل بين العالم وبين المعلوم ، وحصول الإضافة بين الأمرين مسبوقة بتقرير كل واحد منهما ، فوجب أن يكون العلم بالمعلوم مسبوقاً بتقرير ذلك المعلوم في نفسه ، لكن المعلوم إذا كان معدوماً فهو إنما ينتقل إلى الوجود بتأثير القدرة فيه، فيكون حصوله متأخراً عن تأثير القدرة فيه ، الذي هو مشروط بكونه عالماً به. فيلزم وقوع الدور وهو محال .
The third argument: That we say: It has been established that knowledge (ʿilm) means nothing but a specific relation and specific addition occurring between the knower and the known. The occurrence of the relation between two things is preceded by the establishment (taqrīr) of each of them. Therefore, knowledge of the known must be preceded by the establishment of that known in itself. But if the known is non-existent, it only transitions to existence by the effect of power in it. So its occurrence is posterior to the effect of power in it, which is conditioned by His being knowledgeable of it. Thus a vicious circle occurs, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The objector defines knowledge as a relation (nīsba) between a knower and a known. For a relation to exist, both relata must be established (muqarrar). Therefore, knowledge of a thing requires that the known thing be established in itself. But if the known is non-existent, it comes into existence only through God's power. However, God's power to bring it into existence is conditioned by His knowledge of it. This creates a vicious circle: knowledge requires existence, but existence requires knowledge. Such a circle is impossible (muḥāl). Therefore, knowledge of non-existent particulars is impossible.

Key Terms
علم
نسبة
إضافة
تقرير
دور
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge and power are simultaneous in God, so no circle arises.
Correction: The objection is about logical priority, not temporal priority. Even if simultaneous in reality, the argument shows that if knowledge is a relation, then logically the known must be established before it can be known, yet the known's existence depends on power which depends on knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the third objection claim that knowledge of a non-existent thing leads to a vicious circle?
A) Because knowledge is eternal and non-existence is temporal.
B) Because knowledge requires the known to be established, but the non-existent becomes existent only through power, which is conditioned by knowledge.
C) Because God cannot know what does not exist.
D) Because the knower and known must be the same.

Key Takeaway
This passage presents a key objection: if divine knowledge is defined as a relation, then knowing non-existent particulars leads to an impossible vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your understanding of God's knowledge of you depend on your own actions or existence? If you believe God knows you only after you exist or act, how might that create a 'vicious circle' in your reliance on Him? How can you shift your perspective to see His knowledge as unconditioned?
?
This lesson shows a logical flaw in thinking of knowledge as a relation requiring the known to be 'established' first. Have you ever thought that your deeds establish who you are before God knows you? What would change in your trust in God if you believed He knows you intimately before you do anything?
?
The objector claims that if power to bring things into existence is conditioned by knowledge, and knowledge requires the existence of the known, then nothing can ever be known or created. In your own life, do you sometimes feel stuck in a cycle where you need to know something before you act, but you need to act to gain knowledge? How can you break free from that cycle by trusting in a knowledge that precedes all action?

Impossibility of Infinite Regress in Divine Knowledge
Proof that infinite regress in God's self-knowledge entails infinite simultaneous causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of self-referential knowledge in God is impossible on rational grounds.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of dependent causes cannot exist simultaneously.

This lesson examines a powerful argument from classical kalam against the possibility of an actual infinite regress in God's self-knowledge. The argument reveals why infinite simultaneous causes are absurd, and what that implies about the nature of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Notice the logical flow from assumption to conclusion.
The proof is concise; every phrase matters.
2
Identify the assumption and the regress
The argument begins by assuming God's knowledge is unlimited. Then it imagines an infinite chain of self-knowledge levels for one object. Pinpoint where the regress is introduced.
3
Understand the dependency chain
Each level of knowledge is said to be 'dependent on the preceding rank'. Grasp why this creates a cause-effect relationship between levels.
This is the key move that leads to the absurdity.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The chain of dependent ranks would entail simultaneous infinite causes and effects—something the author declares false. Reflect on why that conclusion is necessary.
The proof does not deny God's knowledge; it denies the necessity of knowing all possible self-referential levels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نهاية له من المعلومات، فإذا اعتبرنا علمه بمعلوم واحد، نقول: إنه يصح أن يعلم كونه عالماً بذلك المعلوم ويصح أيضاً أن يعلم كونه عالماً بكونه عالماً به، وهكذا في سائر المراتب التي لا نهاية لها، فلو وجب أن يعلم كل ما يصح أن يكون معلوماً في نفسه الحصل في علمه بذلك المعلوم الواحد مراتب لا نهاية لها في العلوم وتكون كل مرتبة أخيرة منها مفرعة على المرتبة المتقدمة، فيلزم حصول علل ومعلولات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة. وذلك باطل.
For He has no end of known things. So if we consider His knowledge of one known thing, we say: it is valid that He knows that He is knowing that known thing, and also valid that He knows that He knows that He knows it, and so on in all the endless ranks. Then if He must know everything that can possibly be known in itself, then in His knowledge of that single known thing there would be infinite ranks of knowledges, and each last rank of them is dependent on the preceding rank, so it would entail the occurrence of infinite causes and effects simultaneously. And that is false.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) demonstrating the impossibility of an infinite regress in God's self-referential knowledge. It begins by positing that God's knowledge is infinite ('no end of known things'). Then it argues: if we take a single known object, God can know that He knows it, and know that He knows that He knows, and so on without end. If God must know all possible objects of knowledge (including these levels), then for that one object there would be infinite levels of knowledge, each level caused by the one before it. This would mean that infinite causes and effects exist simultaneously, which is absurd (bāṭil). The argument thus concludes that the initial assumption (that God must know all possible knowables) must be false, or that the regress itself is impossible. The reasoning relies on the principle that a chain of dependencies cannot be infinite in actuality.

Key Terms
علم
مراتب لا نهاية لها
علل ومعلولات
باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument proves God's knowledge is finite or limited.
Correction: The argument does not claim God's knowledge is finite. It only shows that an actual infinite regress of dependent simultaneous causes is impossible, which refutes the necessity of God knowing every such self-referential level.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about infinite levels of knowledge of a single object?
A) They are possible because God is omnipotent.
B) They would entail infinite simultaneous causes and effects, which is false.
C) They are only potential, not actual.
D) They are limited to a finite number.

Key Takeaway
An infinite chain of dependent causes cannot exist simultaneously; thus, God's knowledge does not require an infinite regress of self-referential acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s knowledge is not a chain of infinite dependencies, what does that reveal about your own attempts to be completely self-aware? Are you willing to let go of the need to have all your thoughts and motivations perfectly mapped in your own mind, and instead trust that God knows you fully and directly?
?
The argument shows that God does not ‘think about His thinking’ in an endless loop. Have you been treating God’s knowledge as if it were limited by the same discursive process as yours? Repent of any mental image you have made of God as ‘figuring things out’ or ‘investigating’ your heart, and instead magnify Him whose knowledge is immediate and without dependence.
?
If an infinite regress of dependent causes is impossible, then what does that mean for your own sense of self-worth? Are you building your identity on an infinite chain of achievements, memories, and self-reflections that never satisfy? Turn from that futile pursuit and ground your identity in the direct, simple, and complete knowledge that God has of you—knowledge that requires no foundation other than His own self.

The Infinite Cannot Be Known Due to Indistinctness
Proof that the infinite cannot be distinct, hence cannot be known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic argument that the infinite cannot be known because it cannot be distinct from other things.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires distinction; the infinite cannot be distinguished.

This lesson presents the second proof from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah, which argues that divine knowledge cannot encompass the infinite because the infinite lacks the distinctness necessary for any act of knowing. The argument is structured as a formal syllogism, with clear premises derived from the nature of distinction and the nature of knowledge. Understanding this proof sharpens our grasp of how theologians handled the relationship between God’s omniscience and the infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
The argument is compact; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the syllogism
Identify the minor premise (ṣughrā) and major premise (kubrā). The conclusion follows from both.
3
Examine the justifications
For each premise, see how the author justifies it. Distinction requires separation and finitude; knowledge requires conceptual separation.
The justifications are as important as the premises.
4
Consider the conclusion
The conclusion is that the infinite cannot be known. Reflect on what this means for divine knowledge.
This argument limits what can be an object of knowledge, not God's power.
5
Review a common misunderstanding
Some might think this denies God's omniscience. The argument defines knowledge as requiring distinction; the infinite, being unlimited, cannot be distinct.
The focus is on the nature of knowledge, not God's capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه ممتازاً عن الغير، وكل معلوم فإنه يجب كونه ممتازاً عن الغير، ينتج أن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه معلوماً، أما الصغرى فظاهرة لأن الامتياز عن الغير إنما يعقل فيما إذا كان كل واحد من المتميزين منفصلاً عن الآخر وخارجاً عنه، والذي خرج عنه غيره وانفصل عنه غيره، يكون محدوداً متناهياً، فالقول بأنه غير متناهي محال، وأما الكبرى فظاهرة، وذلك لأن المعلوم إنما يكون معلوماً إذا كان بحيث يميزه العقل عما ليس هو، إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لامتنع كونه معلوماً، فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه متميزاً عن غيره، وثبت أن كل ما كان معلوماً، فإنه يجب كونه متميزاً عن غيره، ينتج أن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه معلوماً.
The second proof: That which has no end is impossible for it to be distinct from other, and every known thing must be distinct from other, thus that which has no end is impossible for it to be known. As for the minor premise, it is manifest because distinction from other is only conceivable when each of the two distinct things is separate from the other and external to it, and that from which something else has come out and separated from it is limited and finite, so saying that it is infinite is impossible. As for the major premise, it is manifest because a thing is only known when the intellect distinguishes it from what it is not, since if it were not so, it would be impossible for it to be known. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that which has no end is impossible for it to be distinct from other, and it is established that everything that is known must be distinct from other, thus it results that that which has no end is impossible for it to be known.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained syllogistic proof (ḥujja) that the infinite cannot be known because it cannot be distinct. It is structured as a syllogism with a minor premise (ṣughrā) and major premise (kubrā).

Minor premise: The infinite cannot be distinct from something else (imtināʻ imtiyāz). This is justified because distinction (imtiyāz) requires separation and limit; the infinite by definition has no limit, so it cannot be separate.

Major premise: Every known thing must be distinct (mumtāz) from what it is not. Knowing is distinguishing; the intellect separates the object from its negations.

Conclusion: The infinite cannot be known.

This argument rests on the principle that knowledge involves a conceptual separation between the known and the not-known, and the infinite cannot be so separated because it lacks boundaries. Therefore, God cannot know the infinite as an object of distinct knowledge. This argument is directed against those who claim God knows all infinite things.

Key Terms
لا نهاية له
ممتاز
معلوم
امتياز
متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God knows anything infinite, implying God is not omniscient.
Correction: The argument defines knowledge as requiring distinction between the known and not-known. Since the infinite cannot be distinct, it is not an object of knowledge in that sense. This does not limit God's power; it clarifies the nature of knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument claim the infinite cannot be distinct?
A) Because the infinite is too large for the intellect.
B) Because distinction requires separation and finitude, which the infinite lacks.
C) Because God cannot know things that are not caused.
D) Because the infinite has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The passage argues that the infinite cannot be known because it cannot be distinct, using a syllogistic proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one thing in your life you are absolutely certain you 'know'—but have never genuinely distinguished from its opposite or from what it is not? How would you go about making that distinction today?
?
The passage teaches that knowledge requires the intellect to separate the known from its negation. When it comes to your own faith (iman), have you clearly distinguished it from hypocrisy (nifaq) and mere cultural habit? If not, what concrete step will you take to make that distinction real in your heart?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God or yourself that you have never challenged by asking 'How is this distinct from its opposite?' Are you willing to face the discomfort of that examination, and what might you lose if you don't?

The Second Proof: An Infinite Series Cannot Be Known
Proof that infinite cannot be known via syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series cannot be known according to this proof, and how the syllogism's premises lead to that conclusion.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires distinction; infinite things cannot be distinct, so they cannot be known.

This lesson presents the second proof from the text, which argues that an infinite series cannot be known because it cannot be distinguished from other things. The author builds a syllogism with two self-evident premises. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysing. Get a sense of the overall argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'Ama…' (As for…).
2
Identify the syllogism structure
Notice that the author presents a minor premise (infinite cannot be distinct), a major premise (knowledge requires distinction), and a conclusion (infinite cannot be known). Trace how each premise is argued.
3
Reflect on the key concepts
Consider why distinction implies finitude. The author argues that for two things to be distinct, they must be separate and limited. Also, why does knowledge require the intellect to distinguish the known from what it is not?
These premises are presented as self-evident (ẓāhirah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه ممتازاً عن الغير ، وكل معلوم فإنه يجب كونه ممتازاً عن الغير ، ينتج أن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه معلوماً . أما الصغرى فظاهرة لأن الامتياز عن الغير إنما يعقل فيما إذا كان كل واحد من المتميزين منفصلاً عن الآخر وخارجاً عنه ، والذي خرج عنه غيره وانفصل عنه غيره ، يكون محدوداً متناهياً ، فالقول بأنه غير متناهي محال . وأما الكبرى فظاهرة ، وذلك لأن المعلوم إنما يكون معلوماً إذا كان بحيث يميزه العقل عما ليس هو ، إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لامتنع كونه معلوماً . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه متميزاً عن غيره ، وثبت أن كل ما كان معلوماً ، فإنه يجب كونه متميزاً عن غيره ، ينتج أن ما لا نهاية له يمتنع كونه معلوماً .
The second proof: That which has no end is impossible to be distinct from another, and every known thing must be distinct from another, resulting in that which has no end being impossible to be known. As for the minor premise, it is apparent, because distinctness from another is only conceivable when each of the two distinguished things is separate from the other and outside it, and that from which another has come out and separated from it is limited and finite, so the statement that it is infinite is impossible. As for the major premise, it is apparent, and that is because the known thing is only known when it is such that the intellect distinguishes it from what is not it, for if it were not so, it would be impossible for it to be known. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that which has no end is impossible to be distinct from another, and it is established that everything that is known must be distinct from another, resulting in that which has no end being impossible to be known.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ḥujjah al-thāniyah) against the possibility of God knowing an infinite number of things. The argument is a syllogism with two premises. The minor premise states that an infinite thing cannot be distinguished from another. The author argues that distinction requires separation and being external to the other, which implies finitude and limitation. Hence, calling something infinite while also claiming it is distinct is contradictory. The major premise states that every known thing must be distinct from other things, because knowledge requires the intellect to differentiate the known from what is not it. If something were not distinct, it could not be an object of knowledge. The conclusion follows: an infinite thing cannot be known. The author emphasizes that both premises are self-evident (ẓāhirah). This proof is part of a larger discussion on divine knowledge and infinity.

Key Terms
ممتاز
متناهي
معلوم
ما لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that infinite things can be known collectively as a set.
Correction: The proof shows that knowledge requires distinction at the level of individual objects; even a set would need its members to be distinct, which is impossible for an infinite series.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why can an infinite thing not be known?
A) Because the intellect cannot grasp infinite things.
B) Because infinite things have no end, but knowledge requires distinction, and distinction implies finitude.
C) Because God does not know infinite things by choice.
D) Because the major premise is false.

Key Takeaway
An infinite series cannot be known because it lacks the distinction necessary for intellectual apprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to fully comprehend the infinite mercy or wrath of Allah, only to realize you cannot? How does this proof challenge your tendency to rationalize the infinite attributes of Allah as though they were finite? What one attitude will you adopt today to humbly accept that some divine realities are beyond your grasp?
?
Think of a future event you are anxious about: your career, your health, or your children. That future is an infinite series of unknown moments. According to this proof, can you truly 'know' that future? How does this realization call you to stop trying to control the infinite and instead trust the One who does know? What concrete decision will you make this week to surrender your anxiety about the future to Allah?

The Third Proof: Infinite Knowledge Implies Infinite Attributes
The author argues that God’s knowledge of infinite things would entail infinite attributes in His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against God knowing infinite objects, which argues that infinite knowledge would entail infinite attributes in God's essence, and the two ways this entailment is demonstrated.

Core Idea
Infinite objects of knowledge would require infinite attributes in God, which is impossible.

In this passage, the author presents the third proof that God cannot know an infinite number of things. He argues that if God knew infinite objects, His essence would contain infinite attributes—whether relations or mental forms—and that is impossible. The proof relies on two aspects showing the entailment between knowledge and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow through.
Notice that the passage starts with the conclusion and then proves it.
2
Identify the main claim and the two aspects
The passage states that if God knew infinite things, His essence would have infinite attributes; that is impossible, so such knowledge is impossible. Then it gives two aspects (wajhān) to demonstrate the entailment.
The first aspect considers two theories of knowledge; the second uses logical contradiction and conceivability.
3
Understand the first aspect
The first aspect examines two definitions of knowledge: as a relation/attribution (nīsba, idāfa) and as a form matching the object (ṣūra muṭābiqa). Under both, infinite objects produce infinite attributes in the knower.
The author refutes the objection that relations have no external existence by linking them to knowledge itself.
4
Understand the second aspect
The second aspect uses two points: (1) 'He knows this' contradicts 'He does not know this' but not 'He does not know' generally, showing each known object corresponds to a distinct attribute; (2) we can conceive of God knowing one object while unaware of another, also indicating differentiation.
Both points show that each object requires a distinct attribute of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان عالماً بما لا نهاية له ، لحصل في ذاته صفات لا نهاية لها . وهذا محال . فذاك محال . بيان الملازمة من وجهين : الأول : إنا قد بينا أن العلم عبارة عن النسبة الحاصلة ، وعن الإضافة الخاصة ، فلو كانت المعلومات غير متناهية لحصلت في ذاته نسب موجودة غير متناهية . وليس لقائل أن يقول : هذه النسب والإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان ، لأن العلم لما كان عبارة عن هذه النسب وهذه الإضافات لزم من نفيها في الأعيان ، نفي كونه تعالى عالماً . وذلك باطل . فيثبت أنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بما لا نهاية له لحصلت في ذاته موجودات لا نهاية لها . وأيضاً : العلم عند الفلاسفة عبارة عن صور مطابقة للمعلومات ، فلو كان تعالى عالماً بما لا نهاية له ، لزم أن لا يحصل في ذاته صور لا نهاية لها ، فيثبت أن على التقديرين لو كان تعالى عالماً بما لا نهاية له لزم أن يحصل في ذاته صفات لا نهاية لها وهي إما النسب عند من يقول : العلم عبارة عن النسبة المخصوصة ، وأما الصور المطابقة للمعلومات عند من يقول العلم عبارة عن صور مطابقة . الوجه الثاني : في تقرير هذه المقدمة : إن قولنا : إنه عالم بهذا ، يناقضه قولنا : إنه ليس عالماً بهذا ، ولا يناقضه إنه ليس عالماً . وذلك يدل على التغاير . وأيضاً : يمكننا أن نعتقد كونه عالماً بهذا المعلوم ، مع الذهول عن كونه عالماً بذلك المعلوم الآخر ، وذلك أيضاً يوجب التغاير . فيثبت بهذين الوجهين أنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بمعلومات لا نهاية لها ، لكان قد حصل في ذاته صفات لا نهاية لها .
The third proof: If He were knower of what has no end, then there would occur in His essence attributes with no end. And this is impossible, so that is impossible. The demonstration of the entailment from two aspects: The first: We have already shown that knowledge is the relation that occurs and the specific attribution. So if the objects known were infinite, there would occur in His essence existing relations that are infinite. And no one can say: These relations and attributions have no existence in external entities, because since knowledge is these relations and these attributions, denying them in external entities would necessitate denying that He, exalted, is knowing. And that is false. So it is established that if He, exalted, were knower of what has no end, then there would occur in His essence existents with no end. Also: Knowledge according to the philosophers is forms matching the objects known. So if He, exalted, were knower of what has no end, it would necessitate that there occur in His essence forms with no end. So it is established that on both suppositions, if He, exalted, were knower of what has no end, it would necessitate that there occur in His essence attributes with no end—either the relations for one who says knowledge is the specific relation, or the forms matching the objects known for one who says knowledge is matching forms. The second aspect: In establishing this premise: Our saying 'He is knower of this' contradicts our saying 'He is not knower of this', and it does not contradict 'He is not knower' [at all]. That indicates differentiation. Also: We can believe that He is knower of this known thing while being heedless of His being knower of that other known thing, and that also necessitates differentiation. So it is established by these two aspects that if He, exalted, were knower of objects of knowledge with no end, then there would have occurred in His essence attributes with no end.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujjah al-thālithah) against God knowing an infinite number of objects. It first states the conclusion: if God knew infinite things, His essence would contain infinite attributes, which is impossible; therefore, such knowledge is impossible. The author then demonstrates the entailment (bayān al-mulāzamah) from two aspects. The first aspect considers two theories of knowledge: (1) knowledge is a relation or attribution, and (2) knowledge is a mental form corresponding to the object. Under both theories, if the objects known are infinite, the relations or forms in the knower would also be infinite. The author refutes the objection that relations have no external existence by pointing out that knowledge itself is these relations, so denying their existence would deny God's knowledge. The second aspect argues for the multiplicity of knowledge-states: saying 'He knows this' contradicts 'He does not know this,' but not 'He does not know' (generally), showing that each object of knowledge corresponds to a distinct attribute. Also, we can conceive of God knowing one object while being unaware of His knowledge of another, which indicates differentiation. From these two aspects, it follows that infinite known objects would entail infinite attributes in God's essence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a single act of knowledge can cover all objects, making knowledge a simple attribute that does not multiply.
Correction: The author shows that each object of knowledge requires a distinct attribute—either a relation or a mental form—so infinite objects would require infinite attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God knew an infinite number of things, according to the first aspect of the proof?
A) His essence would become finite.
B) His essence would contain infinite relations or forms.
C) His knowledge would be incomplete.
D) He would not need any attributes.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that infinite objects of knowledge would necessitate infinite attributes in God, which is impossible; therefore, God cannot know an infinite number of things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What sin have you been committing as if He does not see it fully? Will you confess it now and seek His forgiveness, knowing He knows every detail?
?
The author proves that infinite objects would lead to infinite attributes, which is impossible. Reflect on your own assumption that God's knowledge is somehow limited or compartmentalized. How can you train your heart to live in constant awareness that He knows every thought, every glance, every intention? What specific step will you take today to align your actions with this reality?
?
Consider the second aspect: our saying 'He knows this' contradicts 'He does not know this.' When you sin, do you act as if there is a 'this' that God does not know? What lie have you believed that made you comfortable in disobedience? How will you replace that lie with the truth that His knowledge encompasses all things without exception?

The Impossibility of Infinite Attributes: First Argument
The author presents the first argument: every existing number is finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first argument shows that an infinite number of attributes cannot exist in God's essence, based on the nature of numbers.

Core Idea
Every existing number is finite because it can increase or decrease.

The author presents the first of two arguments that demonstrate the impossibility of infinite attributes in God's essence. This argument is straightforward: any actual number is subject to increase and decrease, and anything that can increase or decrease is necessarily finite. Since an infinite number of attributes would be an actual number (a count of attributes), it must be finite. This lesson unpacks this simple but powerful logical point.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument: from 'الاول' to 'متناهي'. Notice the conditional structure: if it can increase/decrease, then it is finite.
2
Understand the key premise
The argument hinges on the concept of 'عدد موجود' (existing number). What does it mean for a number to exist? It means there is an actual count of things.
3
Apply the argument to attributes
The number of attributes in God's essence is an existing number. Therefore, it must be subject to increase or decrease, and thus finite. But can God's attributes increase? No, because His perfection is changeless. So the argument shows that an infinite number is impossible.
This argument assumes that any infinite collection would be an actual number, which is debatable, but it's the classical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أن حصول صفات لا نهاية لها في ذاته محال ، من وجهين : الأول : إن كل عدد موجود فإنه قبل الزيادة والنقصان ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناهي . الثاني : إن كل واحد من تلك المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها ، له بعينه أحكام غير متناهية، فإن تلك الماهية مغايرة لكل ما سواها من الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها. والجوهر الفرد يمكن حصوله في أحياز غير متناهية على البدل وفي أوقات متناهية على البدل ، وموصوفاً بصفات غير متناهية على البدل ، فيثبت أنه حصل في كل واحد من أحاد المعلومات أحكام غير متناهية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون عالماً بما لا نهاية له ، لا مرة واحدة، بل مراراً ، لا نهاية لها ، وذلك
As for the demonstration that the occurrence of attributes with no end in His essence is impossible, it is from two aspects: The first: Every existing number is susceptible to increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite. The second: Every single one of those objects of knowledge that have no end has specifically infinite judgments, for that essence is different from every other essence among the essences that have no end. And the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) can occur in infinite places in alternation and in finite times in alternation, and described by infinite attributes in alternation. So it is established that in each one of the units of objects of knowledge there occur infinite judgments. Then it would necessitate that He be knower of what has no end not once but many times with no end, and that
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage provides the demonstration that infinite attributes cannot exist in God's essence, using two arguments. The first argument: Every actual number is subject to increase and decrease, and anything that can increase or decrease is finite. Therefore, an infinite number of attributes is impossible. The second argument is more complex: Each one of the infinite objects of knowledge has infinite judgments (aḥkām) associated with it because each essence is distinct from all other essences. The author invokes the concept of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard) of the mutakallimūn, which can occupy infinite places over time and can be described by infinite attributes alternately. From this, it follows that each individual object of knowledge has infinite judgments, leading to the conclusion that if God knew an infinite number of objects, He would have to know each object in infinite ways, and He would know infinite objects not once but infinite times. This multiplies the infinity, making the impossibility even clearer. The passage ends abruptly with 'and that...' likely continuing to assert the impossibility.

زيادة ونقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite number of attributes is possible if God is infinite.
Correction: The argument shows that an actual infinite number (a count of distinct items) is contradictory because any number can be increased or decreased, implying finitude.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot have attributes.
B) Every existing number can increase or decrease.
C) Attributes are not real.
D) There is no number of attributes.

Key Takeaway
The first argument proves that an infinite number of attributes is impossible because any actual number is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that God's attributes cannot be infinite in number but are infinite in intensity. In what area of your life do you treat your relationship with God as a numbers game (e.g., counting prayers, good deeds)? How will you shift your focus to the quality and sincerity of your worship starting today?
?
You exist, so you are finite. Yet you are sustained by the Infinite. Does the thought that your existence is entirely dependent on a God beyond number humble you? What prideful assumption about your own self-sufficiency will you consciously surrender before your next prayer?

The Impossibility of Infinite Attributes: Second Argument
The author presents the second argument: infinite judgments per object lead to impossible recursion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument, which uses the concept of the indivisible atom and infinite judgments to show that infinite attributes lead to an impossible multiplication of infinities.

Core Idea
Infinite objects of knowledge require infinite judgments, leading to an impossible recursion.

The second argument is more intricate. It considers each object of God's knowledge, which are infinite, and argues that each object has infinite judgments (properties or relations). Using the atomic theory of the mutakallimūn, the author shows that this would require God to have infinite knowledge not once but infinite times, which is absurd. This argument reinforces the conclusion that infinite attributes cannot exist in God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read from 'الثاني' to the end. Identify the main claim and the example of the indivisible atom.
The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is implied.
2
Understand the notion of 'infinite judgments'
Each object of knowledge has a unique essence (māhiyya) distinct from all others. This distinction itself is a judgment (ḥukm). Since there are infinite objects, there are infinite such judgments.
3
Analyze the atom example
The indivisible atom can occupy infinite places over time and can be described by infinite attributes alternately. This demonstrates that an individual entity can have infinite accidental judgments.
The atom example is used to illustrate how infinite judgments can arise from a single object.
4
Draw the conclusion
If each object of knowledge has infinite judgments, then God would know each object in infinite ways. Since there are infinite objects, God would know infinite objects with infinite judgments, leading to an infinite number of acts of knowledge (not once, but infinite times). This is impossible because it would entail an actual infinite of knowings.
The argument aims to show that the assumption of infinite attributes leads to an absurd multiplication of infinities.

The Fifth Doubt: The Argument for Infinite Attributes
The author presents the fifth doubt with its justification and two proofs of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' argument that if God knew infinite things, His essence would contain infinite attributes, and the two proofs they offer for the impossibility of such attributes.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that infinite knowledge would force infinite attributes into God's essence, which they claim is impossible.

In this passage, the author presents the fifth and most elaborate doubt raised against divine omniscience. The opponents argue that if God knew an infinite number of things, His essence would have to contain an infinite number of attributes—either relations or forms—which they claim is impossible. The author then presents two proofs for the impossibility of infinite attributes, including an argument about multiplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage first establishes that infinite knowledge would lead to infinite attributes in God's essence, then provides two proofs that such infinite attributes are impossible.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof states that any existing number is finite because it accepts increase and decrease. Consider why this is meant to show that infinite attributes cannot exist.
4
Analyze the second proof
The second proof argues that each infinite known thing has infinite judgments, leading to multiplication of the infinite, which is impossible because multiplication is a property of the finite.
This is the core of the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العلم عبارة عن صور مطابقة الوجه الثاني : في تقرير هذه المقدمة : إن قولنا : إنه عالم بهذا ، يناقضه قولنا : إنه ليس عالماً بهذا ، ولا يناقضه إنه ليس عالماً . وذلك يدل على التغاير . وأيضاً : يمكننا أن نهاية لها وأما بيان أن حصول صفات لا نهاية لها في ذاته محال ، من وجهين : الأول : إن كل عدد موجود فإنه قبل الزيادة والنقصان ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناهي . الثاني : إن كل واحد من تلك المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها ، له بعينه أحكام غير متناهية، فإن تلك الماهية مغايرة لكل ما سواها من الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها. والجوهر الفرد يمكن حصوله في أحياز غير متناهية على البدل وفي أوقات متناهية على البدل ، وموصوفاً بصفات غير متناهية على البدل ، فيثبت أنه حصل في كل واحد من أحاد المعلومات أحكام غير متناهية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون عالماً بما لا نهاية له ، لا مرة واحدة، بل مراراً ، لا نهاية لها ، وذلك يوجب أن يصير غير المتناهي مضاعفاً مرات غير متناهية ، وذلك محال ، لأن التضعيف عبارة عن ضم شيء إلى شيء آخر ، وضم أحد الشيئين إلى الآخر ، إنما يكون لو كان كل واحد منهما خارجاً عن الآخر ، والشيء الذي يخرج عنه غيره يكون متناهياً فيثبت أن قبول التضعيف والتثنية من خواص المتناهي فغير المتناهي لا يكون قابلاً له . وهذا تمام الكلام في حكاية شبهات المخالفين . واعلم . أن الشبهة الأولى ، وهي قوله : إنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بالجزئيات قبل وقوعها ، لوجب بطلان الربوبية والعبودية ، فهذا إشارة إلى أن القول بالحيز يوجب هذا المحال . وسيأتي الكلام فيه في مسئلة الحيز . وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : المعدوم يمتنع أن يكون معدوماً . فقد أبطلناه بالدلائل القاهرة في أول هذا الفصل. وأما الشبهة الثالثة : وهي أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بالشيء ، فهذا يقتضي وجود أسباب ومسببات لا آخر لها ، ولا يقتضي نفي الأولية عنها . وأما الشبهة الرابعة . وهي قولهم : ما لا نهاية له لا يحصل فيه الامتياز. فجوابه : إن كل واحد منها ممتاز عن غيره ، وذلك يكفي في كون كل واحد منها معلوماً . وأما الشبهة الخامسة : وهي قولهم : إنه يلزم حصول صفات لا نهاية لها في ذاته تعالى فجوابه : إن هذه التعلقات نسب وإضافات غير متناهية وحصول نسب وإضافات غير متناهية لا يكون ممتنعاً بدليل أن الواحد نصف الإثنين وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة . فهذه النسب غير متناهية ، وهي بأسرها حاصلة ، فيثبت أن القول بهذا غير ممتنع . والله أعلم
[If He were] knowing of what has no end, then there would occur in His essence existents with no end. Also: knowledge according to the philosophers is a representation of forms corresponding to the known things. So if He, exalted, were knowing of what has no end, it would be necessary that there occur in His essence forms with no end. Thus it is established that on both suppositions, if He, exalted, were knowing of what has no end, it would be necessary that there occur in His essence attributes with no end, which are either the relations according to the one who says: knowledge is the specific relation, or the forms corresponding to the known things according to the one who says: knowledge is corresponding forms. The second aspect: in establishing this premise: that our saying: He is knowing of this is contradicted by our saying: He is not knowing of this, but is not contradicted by [our saying]: He is not knowing. And that indicates distinction. Also: we can believe that He is knowing of this known thing while being heedless of His being knowing of that other known thing, and that also necessitates distinction. Thus it is established by these two aspects that if He, exalted, were knowing of infinite known things, there would have occurred in His essence infinite attributes. As for the demonstration that the occurrence of infinite attributes in His essence is impossible, from two aspects: The first: Every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite. The second: Every single one of those infinite known things has itself infinite judgments; for that essence is distinct from all other essences that are infinite. And the indivisible atom can occur in infinite locations alternately, and in finite times alternately, and described by infinite attributes alternately. Thus it is established that in every single one of the individual known things there occur infinite judgments. Then it would be necessary that He be knowing of what has no end, not once, but repeatedly, without end, and that necessitates that the infinite become multiplied infinite times, and that is impossible, because multiplication is the combination of one thing with another, and combining one of two things with the other only occurs if each of them is separate from the other, and the thing from which another exits is finite. Thus it is established that accepting multiplication and duality is a property of the finite; so the infinite cannot accept it. And this is the end of the narration of the doubts of the opponents. And know: The first doubt, which is His saying: If He, exalted, were knowing of particulars before they occur, then it would necessitate the nullification of lordship and servitude. This is an indication that the assertion of location necessitates this impossibility. And the discussion about it will come in the issue of location. As for the second doubt: which is their saying: The non-existent cannot be non-existent. We have refuted it with conclusive proofs at the beginning of this chapter. As for the third doubt: which is that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is distinct from knowledge of the thing; this entails the existence of causes and effects with no end, but does not entail the negation of the First from them. As for the fourth doubt: which is their saying: What has no end does not have distinction. Its answer: Each one of them is distinct from the other, and that suffices for each one of them being known. As for the fifth doubt: which is their saying: It necessitates the occurrence of infinite attributes in His essence, exalted. Its answer: These relations are infinite relations and additions, and the occurrence of infinite relations and additions is not impossible, evidenced by the fact that one is half of two, a third of three, and a fourth of four. So these relations are infinite, and all of them occur. Thus it is established that the assertion of this is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the opponents' doubts regarding God's knowledge of infinite things. It begins with the detailed presentation of the fifth doubt, which argues that if God knew infinite things, His essence would contain infinite attributes (either relations or forms), which is impossible. Two aspects support this: first, the logical distinction between knowing each thing separately shows multiplicity; second, the impossibility of infinite attributes in essence is argued from two angles: (1) any existing number is subject to increase and decrease, hence finite, and (2) each infinite known thing has infinite judgments, leading to infinite multiplication of knowledge, which is impossible because multiplication requires finitude. After concluding the narration of the doubts, the author then briefly refutes each doubt: (1) the objection about knowledge of particulars relates to location and will be discussed later; (2) the objection about the non-existent being impossible is already refuted; (3) the objection about knowledge of knowledge leading to infinite regress does not negate the First; (4) the objection about lack of distinction in the infinite is answered by noting that each thing is distinct; (5) the objection about infinite attributes is answered by noting that relations (like half, third, etc.) can be infinite without impossibility. The core idea is that the opponents' arguments fail because infinite relations are not inherently impossible.

Key Terms
علم
صور مطابقة
نسب
تغاير
تضعيف
متناهي
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is the author's own argument against God's knowledge.
Correction: This is the presentation of the opponents' doubt, not the author's position.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof used to show that infinite attributes are impossible?
A) They contradict divine simplicity.
B) Any existing number is subject to increase and decrease, hence finite.
C) They would lead to infinite regress.
D) They are not actual.

Key Takeaway
The opponents argued that infinite knowledge forces infinite attributes into God's essence, which they claimed is impossible because of the finite nature of numbers and the impossibility of multiplying the infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the opponents argue that infinite knowledge would force a complex multiplicity into God's essence. Do you ever think of God's knowledge as limited because you cannot comprehend how He knows everything at once? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in God's perfect, simple knowledge even when your mind cannot grasp it?
?
The argument uses logical proofs to show that infinite attributes cannot exist in the divine essence. Are there areas in your life where you try to limit God by your own logic? What is one area where you need to submit to the fact that God's knowledge is beyond your finite reasoning?
?
The passage ends with the impossibility of multiplying the infinite. How does this truth affect your understanding of God's oneness and His ability to know every detail of your life without being overwhelmed? Will you commit to relying on His comprehensive knowledge rather than your own limited perspective?

Refutation of the First Four Doubts
The author refutes the first four doubts in rapid succession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's brief refutations of the first four doubts regarding God's knowledge of the infinite.

Core Idea
The first four doubts are refuted succinctly: they either rely on misunderstandings or are already settled.

After presenting the fifth doubt in detail, the author turns to refute the remaining objections. The first doubt—that knowledge of particulars would nullify lordship and servitude—is deferred to the discussion of location. The second doubt, that the non-existent cannot be non-existent, has already been refuted. The third doubt concerning infinite regress is shown not to negate the First. The fourth doubt about lack of distinction in the infinite is answered by noting each thing's distinctness.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutations carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how brief each refutation is compared to the earlier detailed presentation.
2
Understand the first doubt
The first doubt claims that knowing particulars before they occur would negate divine lordship. The author says this relates to the issue of location, to be discussed later.
3
Understand the second and third doubts
The second doubt is already refuted; the third doubt about knowledge of knowledge is answered by noting that infinite regress does not negate the First Cause.
4
Understand the fourth doubt
The fourth doubt claims that the infinite lacks distinction. The author answers that each thing is distinct from others, which is sufficient for knowledge.

Refutation of the Fifth Doubt: Infinite Relations Are Possible
The author refutes the fifth doubt by reinterpreting the attributes as relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's core refutation of the fifth doubt: that the supposed 'attributes' are actually relations, and infinite relations are not impossible.

Core Idea
The fifth doubt is refuted by showing that the infinite attributes are mere relations, which can be infinite without contradiction.

Having presented the fifth doubt's argument that infinite attributes would be impossible, the author now delivers the decisive refutation. He argues that what the opponents call 'attributes' are simply relations and additions (nisab wa idafat), and that infinite relations are not inherently impossible. He gives the example of the number one having infinite relations to all numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Note the example given.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between essential attributes and relational attributes. The infinite attributes are only relations, not real attributes that would multiply God's essence.
3
Analyze the example
One is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, and so on infinitely. All these relations exist without causing multiplicity in the number one.
This illustrates how infinite relations can coexist.

وموصوفاً بصفات غير متناهية على البدل ، فيثبت أنه حصل في كل واحد من أحاد المعلومات أحكام غير متناهية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون عالماً بما لا نهاية له ، لا مرة واحدة، بل مراراً ، لا نهاية لها ، وذلك يوجب أن يصير غير المتناهي مضاعفاً مرات غير متناهية ، وذلك محال ، لأن التضعيف عبارة عن ضم شيء إلى شيء آخر ، وضم أحد الشيئين إلى الآخر ، إنما يكون لو كان كل واحد منهما خارجاً عن الآخر ، والشيء الذي يخرج عنه غيره يكون متناهياً فيثبت أن قبول التضعيف والتثنية من خواص المتناهي فغير المتناهي لا يكون قابلاً له . وهذا تمام الكلام في حكاية شبهات المخالفين . واعلم . أن الشبهة الأولى ، وهي قوله : إنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بالجزئيات قبل وقوعها ، لوجب بطلان الربوبية والعبودية ، فهذا إشارة إلى أن القول بالحيز يوجب هذا المحال . وسيأتي الكلام فيه في مسئلة الحيز . وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : المعدوم يمتنع أن يكون معدوماً . فقد أبطلناه بالدلائل القاهرة في أول هذا الفصل. وأما الشبهة الثالثة : وهي أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بالشيء ، فهذا يقتضي وجود أسباب ومسببات لا آخر لها ، ولا يقتضي نفي الأولية عنها . وأما الشبهة الرابعة . وهي قولهم : ما لا نهاية له لا يحصل فيه الامتياز. فجوابه : إن كل واحد منها ممتاز عن غيره ، وذلك يكفي في كون كل واحد منها معلوماً . وأما الشبهة الخامسة : وهي قولهم : إنه يلزم حصول صفات لا نهاية لها في ذاته تعالى فجوابه : إن هذه التعلقات نسب وإضافات غير متناهية وحصول نسب وإضافات غير متناهية لا يكون ممتنعاً بدليل أن الواحد نصف الإثنين وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة . فهذه النسب غير متناهية ، وهي بأسرها حاصلة ، فيثبت أن القول بهذا غير ممتنع . والله أعلم
[If He were] knowing of what has no end, then there would occur in His essence existents with no end. Also: knowledge according to the philosophers is a representation of forms corresponding to the known things. So if He, exalted, were knowing of what has no end, it would be necessary that there occur in His essence forms with no end. Thus it is established that on both suppositions, if He, exalted, were knowing of what has no end, it would be necessary that there occur in His essence attributes with no end, which are either the relations according to the one who says: knowledge is the specific relation, or the forms corresponding to the known things according to the one who says: knowledge is corresponding forms. The second aspect: in establishing this premise: that our saying: He is knowing of this is contradicted by our saying: He is not knowing of this, but is not contradicted by [our saying]: He is not knowing. And that indicates distinction. Also: we can believe that He is knowing of this known thing while being heedless of His being knowing of that other known thing, and that also necessitates distinction. Thus it is established by these two aspects that if He, exalted, were knowing of infinite known things, there would have occurred in His essence infinite attributes. As for the demonstration that the occurrence of infinite attributes in His essence is impossible, from two aspects: The first: Every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite. The second: Every single one of those infinite known things has itself infinite judgments; for that essence is distinct from all other essences that are infinite. And the indivisible atom can occur in infinite locations alternately, and in finite times alternately, and described by infinite attributes alternately. Thus it is established that in every single one of the individual known things there occur infinite judgments. Then it would be necessary that He be knowing of what has no end, not once, but repeatedly, without end, and that necessitates that the infinite become multiplied infinite times, and that is impossible, because multiplication is the combination of one thing with another, and combining one of two things with the other only occurs if each of them is separate from the other, and the thing from which another exits is finite. Thus it is established that accepting multiplication and duality is a property of the finite; so the infinite cannot accept it. And this is the end of the narration of the doubts of the opponents. And know: The first doubt, which is His saying: If He, exalted, were knowing of particulars before they occur, then it would necessitate the nullification of lordship and servitude. This is an indication that the assertion of location necessitates this impossibility. And the discussion about it will come in the issue of location. As for the second doubt: which is their saying: The non-existent cannot be non-existent. We have refuted it with conclusive proofs at the beginning of this chapter. As for the third doubt: which is that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is distinct from knowledge of the thing; this entails the existence of causes and effects with no end, but does not entail the negation of the First from them. As for the fourth doubt: which is their saying: What has no end does not have distinction. Its answer: Each one of them is distinct from the other, and that suffices for each one of them being known. As for the fifth doubt: which is their saying: It necessitates the occurrence of infinite attributes in His essence, exalted. Its answer: These relations are infinite relations and additions, and the occurrence of infinite relations and additions is not impossible, evidenced by the fact that one is half of two, a third of three, and a fourth of four. So these relations are infinite, and all of them occur. Thus it is established that the assertion of this is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the opponents' doubts regarding God's knowledge of infinite things. It begins with the detailed presentation of the fifth doubt, which argues that if God knew infinite things, His essence would contain infinite attributes (either relations or forms), which is impossible. Two aspects support this: first, the logical distinction between knowing each thing separately shows multiplicity; second, the impossibility of infinite attributes in essence is argued from two angles: (1) any existing number is subject to increase and decrease, hence finite, and (2) each infinite known thing has infinite judgments, leading to infinite multiplication of knowledge, which is impossible because multiplication requires finitude. After concluding the narration of the doubts, the author then briefly refutes each doubt: (1) the objection about knowledge of particulars relates to location and will be discussed later; (2) the objection about the non-existent being impossible is already refuted; (3) the objection about knowledge of knowledge leading to infinite regress does not negate the First; (4) the objection about lack of distinction in the infinite is answered by noting that each thing is distinct; (5) the objection about infinite attributes is answered by noting that relations (like half, third, etc.) can be infinite without impossibility. The core idea is that the opponents' arguments fail because infinite relations are not inherently impossible.

Key Terms
تعلقات
نسب وإضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite relations would multiply God's essence.
Correction: Infinite relations do not cause multiplicity in the subject, as shown by the number one.

Checkpoint

A) A number being infinitely divisible.
B) One being half of two, a third of three, etc.
C) The infinite series of integers.
D) The relation of a part to the whole.

Key Takeaway
The fifth doubt is refuted by showing that the supposed attributes are merely relations, and infinite relations are possible without contradiction.

?
If God's infinite relations do not affect His essence, how should that change the way you approach Him in worship? Do you sometimes feel that because He knows everything, He is distant or impersonal? How does this lesson call you to draw near to a God whose knowledge is perfectly united with His essence?
?
