Refutation of Five Doubts About Divine Knowledge
The author refutes five doubts about divine knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes five specific doubts raised against the doctrine of God's knowledge of particulars, and why infinite relations are not impossible.

Core Idea
Infinite relations are possible; objections about divine knowledge fail.

Opponents of divine knowledge of particulars raise several logical objections. The author, in this passage, systematically dismantles each one, from the claim that it would nullify lordship to the fear of infinite attributes. As we read, notice how he uses examples from mathematics to show that infinite relations are conceptually harmless.

Guided Path
1
Read the preliminary argument
The passage begins by explaining why the infinite cannot be multiplied—a key principle. Then the five doubts are presented. Read the entire passage once.
2
First doubt: Lordship and servitude
The first doubt claims that if God knew particulars before they occur, it would nullify lordship and servitude. The author says this only follows if one posits spatial location for God, which will be discussed later.
3
Second doubt: The non-existent
The second doubt states that the non-existent cannot be non-existent (a paradox). The author says it was already refuted earlier.
4
Third and fourth doubts
The third doubt leads to infinite regress of causes; the author says it does not negate a First. The fourth doubt says the infinite has no distinction; the author says each part is distinct inherently.
5
Fifth doubt: Infinite attributes
The fifth doubt claims that God's knowledge requires infinite attributes. The author responds that these are mere relations, and gives the mathematical example to show infinite relations are possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجب أن يصير غير المتناهي مضاعفاً مرات غير متناهية، وذلك محال، لأن التضعيف عبارة عن ضم شيء إلى شيء آخر، وضم أحد الشيئين إلى الآخر، إنما يكون لو كان كل واحد منهما خارجاً عن الآخر، والشيء الذي يخرج عنه غيره يكون متناهياً فيثبت أن قبول التضعيف والتثنية من خواص المتناهي فغير المتناهي لا يكون قابلاً له. وهذا تمام الكلام في حكاية شبهات المخالفين. واعلم أن الشبهة الأولى، وهي قوله: إنه تعالى لو كان عالماً بالجزئيات قبل وقوعها، لوجب بطلان الربوبية والعبودية، فهذا إشارة إلى أن القول بالحيز يوجب هذا المحال. وسيأتي الكلام فيه في مسئلة الحيز. وأما الشبهة الثانية: وهي قولهم: المعدوم يمتنع أن يكون معدوماً. فقد أبطلناه بالدلائل القاهرة في أول هذا الفصل. وأما الشبهة الثالثة: وهي أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء مغاير للعلم بالشيء، فهذا يقتضي وجود أسباب ومسببات لا آخر لها، ولا يقتضي نفي الأولية عنها. وأما الشبهة الرابعة: وهي قولهم: ما لا نهاية له لا يحصل فيه الامتياز. فجوابه: إن كل واحد منها ممتاز عن غيره، وذلك يكفي في كون كل واحد منها معلوماً. وأما الشبهة الخامسة: وهي قولهم: إنه يلزم حصول صفات لا نهاية لها في ذاته تعالى فجوابه: إن هذه التعلقات نسب وإضافات غير متناهية، وحصول نسب وإضافات غير متناهية لا يكون ممتنعاً بدليل أن الواحد نصف الإثنين وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة. فهذه النسب غير متناهية، وهي بأسرها حاصلة، فيثبت أن القول بهذا غير ممتنع. والله أعلم
[That] necessitates that the infinite becomes multiplied infinite times, and that is impossible, because multiplication is the attachment of one thing to another, and the attachment of one of the two things to the other is only possible if each of them is external to the other, and the thing from which another is external is finite; thus it is established that the acceptance of multiplication and duplication is a property of the finite, so the infinite cannot accept it. And this completes the narration of the doubts of the opponents. And know that the first doubt, which is His saying: If He, exalted, were knowing of particulars before their occurrence, then the nullification of lordship and servitude would be necessary — this indicates that the affirmation of spatial location (al-ḥayyiz) necessitates this impossibility. And the discussion about it will come in the issue of spatial location (al-ḥayyiz). As for the second doubt: which is their saying: The non-existent (al-maʿdūm) is impossible to be non-existent. We have already refuted it with conclusive proofs at the beginning of this chapter. As for the third doubt: which is that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of the thing — this necessitates the existence of causes and effects without end, and it does not necessitate the denial of the First of them. As for the fourth doubt: which is their saying: That which has no end has no distinction [between its parts]. Its answer: Each one of them is distinct from the other, and that suffices for each of them to be known. As for the fifth doubt: which is their saying: It necessitates that there occur infinite attributes in His essence, exalted. Its answer: These attachments are relations (nisab) and additions (iḍāfāt) that are infinite, and the occurrence of infinite relations and additions is not impossible, as evidenced by the fact that one is half of two, and a third of three, and a fourth of four. These relations are infinite, and they all occur; thus it is established that accepting this is not impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a series of refutations (objection-and-answer format) addressing five doubts (shubahāt) raised by opponents regarding God's knowledge of particulars. The author first establishes a logical principle about infinity: the infinite cannot undergo multiplication because multiplication requires each component to be finite and external to the other. This principle is used to refute a prior doubt, and then the author summarizes the five remaining ones. The first doubt claims that if God knew particulars before they occur, it would nullify lordship and servitude; the author says this only follows if one posits spatial location (al-ḥayyiz) for God, which will be discussed later. The second doubt, that the non-existent cannot be non-existent (a self-contradiction), was already refuted earlier. The third doubt argues that knowledge of knowledge of a thing differs from knowledge of the thing itself, leading to an infinite regress of causes; the author points out that this regress does not negate a First Cause. The fourth doubt says that the infinite cannot have distinction (imtiyāz) among its parts; the author replies that each part is distinct from others inherently, so knowledge of them is possible. The fifth doubt claims that God's knowledge requires infinite attributes in His essence; the author responds that these are merely relations and additions (nisab wa iḍāfāt), not actual attributes, and that infinite relations are possible, as shown by the mathematical example: the number one has infinite relations (half of two, third of three, etc.). Thus the objections are all refuted.

Correction: The author demonstrates that infinite relations are possible, as shown by the example of the number one having infinite mathematical relations (half of two, third of three, etc.).

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you live today as if every moment is known and accounted for?

The author refutes the doubt that God's knowledge of particulars would negate lordship and servitude. In your own life, have you ever acted as if God's knowledge of your private thoughts and future choices somehow diminishes your responsibility? How does this lesson correct that assumption?
?
The mathematical example shows that one number has infinite relations—half of two, third of three, etc. If a finite one can have infinite relations, how much more does the Creator have? Three Aspects of Discussing God's Will
Author classifies the discussion on divine will into three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-fold structure that organizes the discussion of the divine attribute of will in this chapter.

Core Idea
The discussion of God's will has three essential aspects: meaning, evidence, and refutation of deniers.

Before diving into the intricate debates about the divine will, the author sets out a roadmap. He divides the discussion into three clear aspects: defining what will means, presenting the proofs for God possessing it, and examining the arguments of those who deny it. This passage provides the overall structure that will guide the rest of the chapter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The Arabic includes a garbled chapter title; focus on the three aspects.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage lists three aspects. Find each one and note how they are introduced with 'الأول', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
3
Reflect on the logical order
Consider why the author begins with the meaning of will, then evidence, then deniers' proofs. This order is systematic: define, prove, then refute objections.
This ordering is common in classical theological works.

Defining Divine Will: Philosophers vs. Theologians
Author presents and contrasts two definitions of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the philosophers' and theologians' definitions of divine will and why the former cannot be applied to God.

Core Idea
Divine will is not an emotional inclination but a neutral preponderating attribute.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah introduces a fundamental debate in Islamic theology: what does it mean for God to will? The philosophers define will as a human-like inclination toward benefit, but the theologians propose a different definition that avoids anthropomorphism. As you read, notice how the argument begins with human experience and then moves to divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Note the structure: philosophers' claim, critique, alternative meaning, theologians' definition, proof announcement.
The passage ends abruptly at 'two matters' — the proofs themselves are not given here.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition
They define will as an inclination to attract benefit and aversion as inclination to repel harm. Reflect on whether this is suitable for God.
This definition is based on human psychological experience.
3
Understand why it fails for God
The passage argues that such desires imply susceptibility to pleasure and pain, which is impossible for God. This is a key point about divine transcendence.
God is beyond benefit and harm.
4
Grasp the theologians' definition
Will is an attribute that makes one of two possibilities preponderate without necessity or compulsion. This is a neutral, non-emotional capacity.
This definition avoids anthropomorphism.
5
Note the introduction to proofs
The passage says there are two proofs for this attribute. Even though the proofs are not given here, this sets up the next stage of the argument.
The next lesson may cover these proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث عن حقيقة الإرادة
(وفي هذا البحث مسائل :
المسألة الأولى) : قالت الفلاسفة : إنا نجد من أنفسنا : أنا إذا تصورنا : أن لنا في الفعل الفلاني منفعة خالصة أو راجحة ، حصل في نفوسنا ميل إلى تحصيل تلك المنافع ، إذا تصورنا : أن لنا في الفعل الآخر مضرة خالصة ، أو راجحة حصل في نفوسنا ميل إلى الدفع والمنع . ونحن سمينا الميل إلى الجذب والتحصيل بالإرادة ، وسمينا الميل إلى الدفع والمنع بالكراهية . وهذا القدر معلوم ، فإن كان المراد من الإرادة والكراهية هذا ، فهذا ممتنع الثبوت في حق الله تعالى ، لأن هذا إنما يعقل ثبوته في حق من يصح عليه اللذة والألم والمنفعة والمضرة، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال ، فكان إثبات الرغبة في جلب المنافع والنفرة عن وصول المضار في حق الله تعالى محالاً . وهذا إذا أريد بالإرادة والكراهية هذا المعنى وأما ، إذا أريد بهما معنى آخر فلا بد فيه من إفادة تصوره ، لننظر فيه أنه هل يصح ذلك في حق الله تعالى أم لا ؟ قال المتكلمون : الإرادة صفة تقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر من غير وجوب ، ومن غير تكوين . هذا هو المراد من صفة الإرادة . والذي يدل على أن هذه الصفة موجودة أمران :
The inquiry into the reality of will. (In this inquiry are issues: the first issue): The philosophers said: We find from ourselves that when we imagine that in a certain action there is a pure or preponderant benefit, there arises in ourselves an inclination to obtain those benefits; and when we imagine that in another action there is a pure or preponderant harm, there arises in ourselves an inclination to repel and prevent. And we call the inclination to attract and obtain 'will', and we call the inclination to repel and prevent 'aversion'. And this much is known. If will and aversion are meant in this sense, then their existence is impossible with respect to God Most High, because this can only be understood to exist in one for whom pleasure, pain, benefit, and harm are possible, and that is impossible with respect to God Most High. So affirming desire to bring benefits and aversion to the arrival of harms in the case of God Most High is impossible. And this is if will and aversion are intended in this meaning. But if another meaning is intended by them, then it is necessary to convey its conception so that we may consider whether it is valid with respect to God Most High or not. The theologians said: Will is an attribute that necessitates the preponderance of one of the two possibilities over the other without necessity and without compulsion. This is what is meant by the attribute of will. And that which indicates that this attribute exists are two matters:
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a definitional discussion about the nature of divine will. It first reports the philosophers' definition: they define will as an inclination toward benefit and aversion as an inclination away from harm, based on human experience. The author then notes that if such desires are what is meant by will and aversion, they cannot be attributed to God because God is beyond pleasure, pain, benefit, and harm. Thus, affirming a desire for benefit or repulsion from harm in God is impossible. The author then opens the possibility that the terms might have another meaning, which must be clarified. Next, the theologians' definition is given: will is an attribute that makes one of two possible outcomes preponderate without necessity or external compulsion. The passage concludes by stating that there are two proofs for the existence of this attribute, but the text cuts off before enumerating them. The author's order is: (1) philosophers' claim, (2) its refutation for God, (3) the need for an alternative meaning, (4) the theologians' definition, (5) introduction to proofs.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الكراهية

المتكلمون

ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will is an emotional inclination like human desire for benefit.
Correction: Divine will is an attribute that preponderates one possibility without passion or need, as defined by the theologians.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians (المتكلمون), what is the definition of will (الإرادة)?
A) An inclination toward benefit and away from harm.
B) A quality that necessitates the preponderance of one of two possibilities without necessity or compulsion.
C) A quality that compels action out of necessity.
D) A desire for pleasure and aversion to pain.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' definition of will as desire for benefit cannot apply to God; the theologians define it as a neutral preponderating attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation you desperately want to change, do you ever assume that God’s will must align with your own sense of benefit? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to a will that is not driven by need but by pure wisdom?
?
The philosophers defined will as a desire for benefit—something we naturally feel. Can you identify areas in your life where you still unconsciously project this onto God, expecting Him to act out of a need to give you pleasure or spare you pain? How can you begin to uproot this assumption?
?
If God’s will is a neutral preponderating attribute, then every event in your life—whether painful or joyful—is chosen with no compulsion and no necessity. How does this truth compel you to trust even what you cannot understand, and to actively reframe your complaints as opportunities for deeper reliance?

The Reduction of God's Will to Knowledge
The author presents the first theological position on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite view that God's will is identical to His knowledge of benefit, and why they exclude 'motive of need' for God.

Core Idea
Some Mu'tazilites reduce God's will to mere knowledge of what is beneficial.

The passage begins by presenting the first major position on God's will: that it is nothing but God's knowledge of the benefit of an act. This view is attributed to three prominent Mu'tazilite thinkers. Before diving into the more complex classifications, it is essential to grasp this reductive account, which sparked the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the first two sentences
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'وهذا هو داعية الحاجة'. Notice how the definition of will is given as knowledge of benefit.
The passage uses technical terms like 'راجح المنفعة'.
2
Understand the two motives
Identify the distinction between 'داعية الحاجة' (motive of need) and 'داعية الإحسان' (motive of beneficence). Why is the first impossible for God?
3
Apply to God
See that only the motive of beneficence remains for God. This is the core of their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية: اختلف الناس في إثبات كونه مريداً. قال الكعبي والجاحظ وأبو الحسين البصري: معنى كونه تعالى مريداً للفعل: علمه يكون ذلك الفعل راجح المنفعة في حقه. وهذا هو داعية الحاجة. وأما إن اعتقد كونه راجح المنفعة في حق الغير فهو داعية الإحسان. والأول في حق الله تعالى محال، فبقي الداعي في حق الله تعالى هو القسم الثاني. وقال الباقون من المتكلمين: معنى الإرادة في حق الله تعالى صفة زائدة على ذلك العلم. ثم اختلفوا فيها على وجوه مختلفة. وضبط الأقوال أن يقال: الإرادة إما أن تكون صفة سلبية، أو إيجابية. فالذين قالوا: إنها صفة سلبية، قالوا: معنى كونه مريداً: إنه فعل ذلك الفعل لا على سبيل القهر والإكراه. وأما الذين قالوا: إنها صفة إيجابية مغايرة لذلك العلم. منهم من قال: إن ذاته تعالى توجب تلك المريدية. ومنهم من قال: إن حصل معنى ذلك المعنى توجب المريدية. ثم المختلفوا. فقال بعضهم: ذلك المعنى الموجب: صفة قديمة أزلية ممتنعة التبدل والزوال. وقال آخرون: ذلك المعنى حادث. ثم ذلك المعنى. منهم من قال: إنه يحدث في ذات الله تعالى، وهم الكرامية، ومنهم من قال: إنه يحدث لا في محل وهم قوم عظيم من المعتزلة. وأما القسم الثالث، وهو أنه يحدث في غير ذات الله تعالى فيوجب الله تعالى صفة المريدية، فهذا قول لم يقل به أحد. وهذا تفصيل أقوال الناس في هذا الباب
The second issue: People differed on establishing that He is willing. Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said: The meaning of His being willing of an act is His knowledge that that act is preponderant in benefit with respect to Him. And this is the motive of need. As for if He believes it to be preponderant in benefit with respect to another, it is the motive of beneficence. The first is impossible with respect to God most high, so the motive with respect to God most high remains the second category. The rest of the mutakallimun said: The meaning of will with respect to God most high is an attribute additional to that knowledge. Then they differed concerning it in various ways. The control of the statements is that it is said: Will is either a negative attribute or a positive attribute. Those who said it is a negative attribute said: The meaning of His being willing is that He did that act not by way of force and coercion. As for those who said it is a positive attribute distinct from that knowledge: some of them said that His essence causes that state of being willing. And some of them said that if a certain meaning obtains, that meaning causes the state of being willing. Then they differed. Some said: that causing meaning is an eternal, pre-eternal attribute impossible to change and perish. Others said: that meaning is originated. Then that meaning: some said it occurs in the essence of God most high—these are the Karramiyya; and some said it occurs not in a locus—these are a great group of the Mu'tazila. As for the third division, namely that it occurs in other than God's essence and then God most high causes the attribute of being willing—this is a statement that no one has held. And this is the detailed account of people's statements in this chapter.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a classification of theological opinions on the meaning of God's will. The author first states the issue: people differed on establishing that God is willing (muriid). Then he lists the views. Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduce God's will to His knowledge that an act is preponderant in benefit for Himself (motive of need) or for others (motive of beneficence); since need is impossible for God, only the second remains. The majority of mutakallimun say will is an additional attribute beyond knowledge. The author then organizes the various positions. Some say will is a negative attribute: God acts without coercion. Others say it is positive: some hold that God's essence directly entails being willing; others hold that a separate meaning (ma'na) causes the state of being willing. Among those who posit a meaning, some say it is eternal (the Ash'aris and others), some say it is originated (hadith). Those who say it is originated differ: the Karramiyya say it occurs in God's essence; a group of Mu'tazila say it occurs not in a locus. The author notes that no one holds that it occurs in a locus other than God. This classification systematically maps the logical possibilities of the attribute.

Key Terms
إرادة
داعية الحاجة
داعية الإحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, what is the meaning of God's will?
A) God's power to create
B) God's knowledge that an act is beneficial
C) God's act of volition
D) God's command

Key Takeaway
For some Mu'tazilites, God's will is simply His knowledge of what is beneficial for others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you instinctively assume that God's will is only what is beneficial for you? How does this lesson challenge you to trust God's will even when you cannot see the benefit?
?
Have you ever reduced your relationship with God to mere intellectual assent—believing correct facts about Him instead of submitting to His will? What would it mean for you to truly embrace God's will as more than just knowledge?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that God's will is always for the benefit of others. Does your life reflect a similar selflessness? Where are you acting out of 'motive of need' rather than 'motive of beneficence'?

Divine Attributes

Will as an Additional Attribute: Negative and Positive Views
The author classifies the majority views on God's will into negative and positive, then subdivides positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between negative and positive interpretations of God's will, and the various sub-positions on how a positive will relates to God's essence.

Core Idea
Theologians divide on whether God's will is a negative or positive attribute, with further splits on its nature.

After presenting the reductive view, the author introduces the majority position: God's will is an attribute distinct from knowledge. He then organizes the various opinions into a logical tree, distinguishing between negative and positive interpretations. This classification reveals the rich diversity of kalam thought on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the majority premise
Read from 'وقال الباقون من المتكلمين' to 'على وجوه مختلفة'. Note that the majority holds will is an additional attribute.
2
Identify the two broad categories
Find the sentence 'الإرادة إما أن تكون صفة سلبية، أو إيجابية'. Understand the negative view: acting without coercion.
3
Explore the positive views
Trace the subdivisions: some say essence causes wilfulness, others say a meaning causes it. Then the meaning is either eternal or originated.
The Karramiyya say the meaning occurs in God's essence; the Mu'tazila say without locus.
4
Note the empty possibility
Read the statement about the third division that no one holds. This shows the logical completeness of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second issue: People differed on establishing that He is willing. Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said: The meaning of His being willing of an act is His knowledge that that act is preponderant in benefit with respect to Him. And this is the motive of need. As for if He believes it to be preponderant in benefit with respect to another, it is the motive of beneficence. The first is impossible with respect to God most high, so the motive with respect to God most high remains the second category. The rest of the mutakallimun said: The meaning of will with respect to God most high is an attribute additional to that knowledge. Then they differed concerning it in various ways. The control of the statements is that it is said: Will is either a negative attribute or a positive attribute. Those who said it is a negative attribute said: The meaning of His being willing is that He did that act not by way of force and coercion. As for those who said it is a positive attribute distinct from that knowledge: some of them said that His essence causes that state of being willing. And some of them said that if a certain meaning obtains, that meaning causes the state of being willing. Then they differed. Some said: that causing meaning is an eternal, pre-eternal attribute impossible to change and perish. Others said: that meaning is originated. Then that meaning: some said it occurs in the essence of God most high—these are the Karramiyya; and some said it occurs not in a locus—these are a great group of the Mu'tazila. Commentary
This passage is a classification of theological opinions on the meaning of God's will. The author first states the issue: people differed on establishing that God is willing (muriid). Then he lists the views. Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduce God's will to His knowledge that an act is preponderant in benefit for Himself (motive of need) or for others (motive of beneficence); since need is impossible for God, only the second remains. Others say it is positive: some hold that God's essence directly entails being willing; others hold that a separate meaning (ma'na) causes the state of being willing. Among those who posit a meaning, some say it is eternal (the Ash'aris and others), some say it is originated (hadith). Those who say it is originated differ: the Karramiyya say it occurs in God's essence; a group of Mu'tazila say it occurs not in a locus. The author notes that no one holds that it occurs in a locus other than God. This classification systematically maps the logical possibilities of the attribute.

Which group held that the originated meaning of will occurs in God's essence?
A) The Mu'tazila

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will, do you instinctively imagine it as something 'added' to God or as simply the way He acts? Which of these views makes you feel closer to understanding His freedom, and which makes you feel more distant? What does your preference reveal about how you relate to God's commands in your daily choices?
?
The passage shows that some theologians said God's will is 'negative'—just the absence of coercion. Have you ever acted in a way that was merely 'not forced' but without true desire? Can you pinpoint an area in your life where you obey God's will only out of obligation, not genuine longing? What would it take for you to turn that into a positive, willing submission?
?
Some argued that God's will is an eternal, unchangeable attribute; others said it is originated, rising within God or without a locus. Which of these makes you more comfortable, and which unsettles you? Is your comfort based on logic or on a need to control God? Are you willing to sit with the tension that God's will may not fit your categories?

The Reductionist View: Will as Knowledge of Benefit
Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and al-Basri define will as knowledge of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduced God's will to knowledge of benefit.

Core Idea
Some theologians equated God's will with knowledge of what benefits Him or others.

This passage introduces one of the major disagreements in Islamic theology: the meaning of God's will (al-irada). Before examining the majority view, we first encounter a reductionist approach that identifies will with a specific kind of knowledge. Understanding this position is crucial for grasping the theological landscape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the authors' own words set the context.
Notice the naming of three specific theologians.
2
Identify the three theologians
Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri are mentioned. Note that they represent a minority view.
3
Understand the two types of motive
They distinguish between motive of need (benefit to self) and motive of beneficence (benefit to others). The first is impossible for God.
This distinction is key to their definition.
4
Grasp the conclusion
For God, only the motive of beneficence remains, so His will is knowledge of benefit to others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : اختلف الناس في إثبات كونه مريداً . فقال الكعبي والجاحظ وأبو الحسين البصري . معنى تعالى مريداً للفعل : علمه يكون ذلك الفعل راجح المنفعة في حقه . وهذا هو داعية الحاجة . وأما إن اعتقد كونه راجح المنفعة في حق الغير فهو داعية الإحسان . والأول في حق الله تعالى محال ، فبقي الداعي في حق الله تعالى هو القسم الثاني . وقال الباقون من المتكلمين : معنى الإرادة في حق الله تعالى صفة زائدة على ذلك العلم . ثم اختلفوا فيها على وجوه مختلفة . وضبط الأقوال أن يقال : الإرادة إما أن تكون صفة سلبية ، أو إيجابية. فالذين قالوا : إنها صفة سلبية ، قالوا : معنى كونه مريداً : إنه فعل ذلك الفعل لا على سبيل القهر والإكراه . وأما الذين قالوا : إنها صفة إيجابية مغايرة لذلك العلم . منهم من قال : إن ذاته تعالى توجب تلك المريدية . ومنهم من قال : إن حصل معنى ذلك المعنى توجب المريدية . ثم اختلفوا . فقال بعضهم : ذلك المعنى الموجب : صفة قديمة أزلية ممتنعة التبدل والزوال . وقال آخرون : ذلك المعنى حادث . ثم ذلك المعنى . منهم من قال : إنه يحدث في ذات الله تعالى، وهم الكرامية ، ومنهم من قال : إنه يحدث لا في محل وهم قوم عظيم من المعتزلة . وأما القسم الثالث ، وهو أنه يحدث في غير ذات الله تعالى فيوجب الله تعالى صفة المريدية ، فهذا قول لم يقل به أحد. وهذا تفصيل أقوال الناس في هذا الباب
The second issue: People differed in affirming that He is a willer. Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said: The meaning of the Exalted being a willer of the act is His knowledge that that act is preponderant in benefit in His right. This is the motive of need. But if He believes it to be preponderant in benefit in the right of another, it is the motive of beneficence. The first is impossible in the right of God the Exalted, so the motive in the right of God the Exalted remains the second category. The rest of the theologians said: The meaning of will (al-irada) in the right of God the Exalted is an attribute additional to that knowledge. Then they differed regarding it in various ways. The summary of the positions is that it is said: Will (al-irada) is either a negative attribute or a positive one. Those who said it is a negative attribute said: The meaning of His being a willer is that He does that act not by way of coercion and compulsion. As for those who said it is a positive attribute different from that knowledge: among them are those who said that His Essence the Exalted necessitates that willer-ness. And among them are those who said that if a meaning of that meaning occurs, it necessitates the willer-ness. Then they differed. Some said: That necessitating meaning is an eternal, beginningless attribute, impossible to change and cease. Others said: That meaning is originated (hadith). Then regarding that meaning: among them are those who said it occurs in the essence of God the Exalted—they are the Karramiyya; and among them are those who said it occurs not in a locus—they are a great group of the Mu'tazila. As for the third category, that it occurs in other than the essence of God the Exalted, thus God the Exalted necessitates the attribute of willer-ness—this is a saying that no one has held. And this is the detailed account of people's statements in this topic.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the different positions on the meaning of God's will (al-irada). It begins by stating the second issue: the disagreement over affirming that God is a willer (murīd). It then presents three main groups: (1) al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who reduce will to knowledge of benefit; (2) the majority of theologians who hold that will is an additional attribute; (3) a further subdivision within the latter group between those who treat it as a negative attribute (simply the absence of coercion) and those who treat it as a positive attribute. Within the positive-attribute camp, there are further disagreements: some say the Essence itself necessitates willer-ness; others say a separate meaning (ma'nā) necessitates it. Then they differ on whether that meaning is eternal or originated. Among those who take it as originated, some locate it in God's essence (Karramiyya) and others in no locus (some Mu'tazila). A hypothetical third option (originating outside God) is reported as never held. This passage thus provides a comprehensive map of the theological landscape on this issue.

Key Terms
الإرادة
مريد
داعية الحاجة
داعية الإحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think all theologians defined will identically.
Correction: This passage shows the diversity, including the reductionist view that equates will with knowledge of benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri say is the meaning of God being a willer?
A) A positive attribute additional to knowledge.
B) Knowledge that the act is preponderant in benefit in His right.
C) The absence of coercion.
D) An eternal meaning in God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The reductionist view equates God's will with His knowledge of what is beneficial, distinguishing between benefit to Himself (impossible) and benefit to others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is merely knowledge of benefit, does that make His will impersonal? How does this view affect your understanding of God's personal relationship with you?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you often act out of 'motive of need'? How does the impossibility of such a motive for God challenge you to purify your intentions in worship?
?
This passage reduces God's will to beneficence. Are you willing to trust that everything God wills for you is truly beneficial, even when you cannot see the benefit? What would change in your life if you fully embraced that?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Majority View: Will as an Additional Attribute
Classification of the majority's views on will as an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that God's will is an additional attribute, and the various subdivisions regarding whether it is negative or positive, and its relationship to the divine essence.

Core Idea
The majority of theologians hold that God's will is a distinct attribute, but they disagree on its nature and locus.

After the reductionist view, the rest of the theologians affirm that will is an additional attribute beyond knowledge. This passage presents a comprehensive classification of their positions, including the negative vs positive distinction, and further disagreements on whether the will is eternal or originated, and where it subsists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from the majority view onward
Read from 'وقال الباقون' to the end. Notice the systematic classification.
This passage is a classification tree.
2
Identify the two main categories: negative and positive
First, the majority say will is additional. Then they split into negative (no coercion) and positive (a real attribute).
3
Within positive, note the two sub-positions
Some say essence necessitates willer-ness; others say a separate meaning necessitates it.
This is a key division.
4
Understand the disagreement on the meaning's eternity
Among those who posit a meaning, some say it is eternal, others originated.
5
Recognize the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila positions
For the originated meaning, the Karramiyya say it subsists in God's essence; the Mu'tazila say in no locus. A third option is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : اختلف الناس في إثبات كونه مريداً . فقال الكعبي والجاحظ وأبو الحسين البصري . معنى تعالى مريداً للفعل : علمه يكون ذلك الفعل راجح المنفعة في حقه . وهذا هو داعية الحاجة . وأما إن اعتقد كونه راجح المنفعة في حق الغير فهو داعية الإحسان . والأول في حق الله تعالى محال ، فبقي الداعي في حق الله تعالى هو القسم الثاني . وقال الباقون من المتكلمين : معنى الإرادة في حق الله تعالى صفة زائدة على ذلك العلم . ثم اختلفوا فيها على وجوه مختلفة . وضبط الأقوال أن يقال : الإرادة إما أن تكون صفة سلبية ، أو إيجابية. فالذين قالوا : إنها صفة سلبية ، قالوا : معنى كونه مريداً : إنه فعل ذلك الفعل لا على سبيل القهر والإكراه . وأما الذين قالوا : إنها صفة إيجابية مغايرة لذلك العلم . منهم من قال : إن ذاته تعالى توجب تلك المريدية . ومنهم من قال : إن حصل معنى ذلك المعنى توجب المريدية . ثم اختلفوا . فقال بعضهم : ذلك المعنى الموجب : صفة قديمة أزلية ممتنعة التبدل والزوال . وقال آخرون : ذلك المعنى حادث . ثم ذلك المعنى . منهم من قال : إنه يحدث في ذات الله تعالى، وهم الكرامية ، ومنهم من قال : إنه يحدث لا في محل وهم قوم عظيم من المعتزلة . وأما القسم الثالث ، وهو أنه يحدث في غير ذات الله تعالى فيوجب الله تعالى صفة المريدية ، فهذا قول لم يقل به أحد. وهذا تفصيل أقوال الناس في هذا الباب
The second issue: People differed in affirming that He is a willer. Al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri said: The meaning of the Exalted being a willer of the act is His knowledge that that act is preponderant in benefit in His right. This is the motive of need. But if He believes it to be preponderant in benefit in the right of another, it is the motive of beneficence. The first is impossible in the right of God the Exalted, so the motive in the right of God the Exalted remains the second category. The rest of the theologians said: The meaning of will (al-irada) in the right of God the Exalted is an attribute additional to that knowledge. Then they differed regarding it in various ways. The summary of the positions is that it is said: Will (al-irada) is either a negative attribute or a positive one. Those who said it is a negative attribute said: The meaning of His being a willer is that He does that act not by way of coercion and compulsion. As for those who said it is a positive attribute different from that knowledge: among them are those who said that His Essence the Exalted necessitates that willer-ness. And among them are those who said that if a meaning of that meaning occurs, it necessitates the willer-ness. Then they differed. Some said: That necessitating meaning is an eternal, beginningless attribute, impossible to change and cease. Others said: That meaning is originated (hadith). Then regarding that meaning: among them are those who said it occurs in the essence of God the Exalted—they are the Karramiyya; and among them are those who said it occurs not in a locus—they are a great group of the Mu'tazila. As for the third category, that it occurs in other than the essence of God the Exalted, thus God the Exalted necessitates the attribute of willer-ness—this is a saying that no one has held. And this is the detailed account of people's statements in this topic.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the different positions on the meaning of God's will (al-irada). It begins by stating the second issue: the disagreement over affirming that God is a willer (murīd). It then presents three main groups: (1) al-Ka'bi, al-Jahiz, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who reduce will to knowledge of benefit; (2) the majority of theologians who hold that will is an additional attribute; (3) a further subdivision within the latter group between those who treat it as a negative attribute (simply the absence of coercion) and those who treat it as a positive attribute. Within the positive-attribute camp, there are further disagreements: some say the Essence itself necessitates willer-ness; others say a separate meaning (ma'nā) necessitates it. Then they differ on whether that meaning is eternal or originated. Among those who take it as originated, some locate it in God's essence (Karramiyya) and others in no locus (some Mu'tazila). A hypothetical third option (originating outside God) is reported as never held. This passage thus provides a comprehensive map of the theological landscape on this issue.

الصفة الإيجابية
المعنى
الكرامية
المعتزلة

Misconception: Some may think the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila agreed on the locus of will.
Correction: The passage shows the Karramiyya hold it occurs in God's essence, while the Mu'tazila hold it occurs in no locus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that the originated meaning of will occurs in God's essence?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) The Karramiyya
C) Al-Ka'bi et al.
D) The negative attribute proponents

Key Takeaway
The majority view on God's will is further divided into negative and positive attributes, with the positive subdivided based on whether the will is eternal or originated, and where it subsists.

Have you ever acted as if God's will operates independently of His knowledge, leading you to doubt His wisdom in your trials? What one area of your life do you need to surrender to the reality that His will is always rooted in perfect knowledge?

How does affirming that His will is eternal and unchanging change how you respond to your current circumstances? What practical step will you take today to align your heart with that truth?

The Argument from Specification for the Divine Will
A rational proof for the attribute of will from specification of creation time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specification of the world's time of creation necessitates a distinct divine attribute of will, beyond power and knowledge.

Core Idea
Specification requires a specifier: the divine will.

Theologians have long argued that the world's creation at a particular time, despite equal possibility of other times, demands a cause beyond mere power or knowledge. This lesson unpacks that proof, showing how power and knowledge are insufficient, and why a distinct attribute of will is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting premise
The proof begins with the accepted fact that the world is originated and its existence occurs at a specific time, though other times were equally possible. This creates the need for a specifier (mukhassis).
The premise is that the world is originated; this is not being proven here.
2
Rule out power as the specifier
Read how the proof argues that power (qudra) relates equally to all times, so it cannot specify a particular time. A different cause is needed.
Power is necessary for creation but indifferent to timing.
3
Rule out knowledge as the specifier
The proof presents two possibilities for knowledge as specifier and refutes both: either knowledge of benefit (invalid due to God not acting for purposes) or knowledge of occurrence (leads to circularity).
The circularity argument is subtle: knowledge of occurrence depends on occurrence, which depends on specification.
4
Conclude the need for will
After eliminating power and knowledge, and noting that life, hearing, sight, and speech are unsuitable, the proof concludes that a distinct attribute—will—must effect the specification by way of preponderance without necessitation.
Will is defined as the attribute that preponderates one possible over another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بإثبات هذه الصفة : فقالوا : قد ثبت أن العالم محدث ، فقد حصل وجوده في وقت معين ، مع أنه كان يجوز في العقل حدوثه قبل ذلك أو بعده . فاختصاص حدوثه بذلك الوقت المعين دون ما قبله وما بعده لا بد له من مخصص ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المخصص هو القدرة . لأن القدرة حاصلة للإحداث في جميع الأوقات، ونسبتها إلى الإحداث في كل واحد من تلك الأوقات على السوية ، فهذا المخصص والمرجح لا بد وأن يكون مغايراً لتلك القدرة ، ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المخصص هو العلم ، لأنه إما أن يكون المراد أن علمه بما في الفعل من المصلحة يدعوه إليه ، أو المراد أن علمه بأن الشيء الفلاني نفع يدعوه إلى الفعل ، وأن الشيء الفلاني لا نفع فيه يدعوه إلى الترك . والأول باطل ، لأن كل دليل دلّ على أنه لا يجوز تعليل أفعال الله تعالى بالعلل والأغراض ، فإنه يدل على بطلان هذا القسم . وأما القسم الثاني - فهو أيضاً باطل . لأن العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع ، الذي هو تبع لهذا التخصيص ، فلو عللنا هذا التخصيص بالعلم بالوقوع . لزم الدور وأنه محال ، فيثبت أن هذا التخصيص غير واقع بسبب القدرة ، ولا بسبب العلم ، فلا بد من صفة أخرى مقتضية لهذا التخصيص والترجيح وظاهر أن الحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام لا يصلح لذلك . فلا بد من صفة أخرى سوى هذه الصفات . ثم تأثيرها في التخصيص إن كان على سبيل الوجوب كان المؤثر موجباً بالذات، وإن كان على سبيل الصفة فهو المطلوب ، لأن هذه الصفة صفة تقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، لا على سبيل الإيجاب ، ولا على سبيل التكوين . وذلك هو المطلوب. وهذا تقرير هذا الدليل على أحسن الوجوه
Those who affirm this attribute argued and said: It is established that the world is originated. So its existence occurred at a specific time, although it was possible in the intellect for it to occur before that or after it. So the specification of its occurrence at that particular time rather than before or after it must have a specifier (mukhassis). And it is not permissible that that specifier be power (al-qudra), because power is available for origination at all times, and its relation to origination in each of those times is equal. So this specifier and preponderator must be different from that power. And it is not permissible that that specifier be knowledge, because it is either meant that His knowledge of the benefit in the act calls Him to it, or meant that His knowledge that a certain thing is beneficial calls Him to the act, and that a certain thing has no benefit calls Him to abandonment. The first is invalid, because every proof that proves that it is not permissible to explain God's acts by causes and purposes indicates the invalidity of this category. As for the second category, it is also invalid, because knowledge of occurrence is dependent on occurrence, which is dependent on this specification. So if we explain this specification by knowledge of occurrence, a circularity (al-dawr) would be necessary, which is impossible. Thus it is established that this specification does not occur due to power, nor due to knowledge, so there must be another attribute necessitating this specification and preponderating. And it is apparent that life, hearing, sight, and speech are not suitable for that. So there must be another attribute besides these attributes. Then its effect in specification: if it is by way of necessity, the influencer would be an essential necessitator; if it is by way of attribute, that is what is sought. Because this attribute is an attribute that necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other, not by way of necessitation, nor by way of origination (al-takwin). And that is what is sought. This is the exposition of this proof in the best way.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil) for the existence of the attribute of will (al-irada) in God. It begins from an accepted premise: the world is originated (hadith) and its existence occurs at a specific time, though other times were equally possible. This specification (takhsis) requires a specifier (mukhassis). The proof rules out power (qudra) as that specifier because power relates equally to all times. It also rules out knowledge ('ilm) by showing that any attempt to tie knowledge to specification leads either to unacceptable anthropomorphic motives (invalid because God's acts are not caused by purposes) or to circularity (dawr) because knowledge of occurrence presupposes occurrence. Therefore, a distinct attribute is needed. The proof then dismisses life, hearing, sight, and speech as unsuitable. Finally, it characterizes the required attribute as one that effects preponderance (tarjih) without necessitation (ijab) or origination (takwin), which is precisely the attribute of will.

Key Terms
التخصيص
المخصص
القدرة
العلم
الدور
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof attempts to prove God's existence.
Correction: The proof actually assumes the world's origination (and thus God's existence as Creator) and argues specifically for the existence of the attribute of will.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can power (qudra) not be the specifier of the world's time of creation?
A) Because power is capable of creation at all times equally.
B) Because power is infinite.
C) Because power is an essential attribute.
D) Because power is not distinct from will.

Key Takeaway
The specification of the world's time of creation proves that God possesses a distinct will that preponderates one possibility over others without necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been convinced that God's power alone explains everything He does, or that His knowledge of what is best automatically determines His actions? How does this lesson's separation of power and knowledge from specification challenge that assumption? Will you now consciously acknowledge that God's will is distinct and deliberate in every detail of your life, even when you cannot see the reason?
?
The argument eliminates purposes as explanations for God's acts, affirming that His will is free and not bound by external benefits. Does your heart truly accept that God does not need to explain Himself to you? Reflect on a recent situation where you wished God had acted differently—did your frustration stem from assuming His will should align with your sense of benefit? How will you submit to His unconstrained will from now on?
?
The passage argues that specification cannot be by necessity, meaning God's will is not forced. Are there areas in your life where you treat God's decrees as though they were automatic consequences of His nature, rather than acts of His free will? Will you begin to see each event in your life, including its specific timing, as a direct choice from God's will, and respond with gratitude and trust?

Objection to the Proof of Will and the Response
Objection to the proof of will and the response defending the need for a distinct attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection to the proof of divine will and the beginning of the response, including the challenge that a non-necessary influencer is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
An objection challenges the coherence of a non-necessary influencer, defending the need for a distinct attribute.

This passage presents a critical objection to the earlier proof that divine will is necessary. The objector argues that an influencer that acts without necessitation or origination is rationally impossible, and suggests that power could serve as the specifier. The response begins by addressing the objection, setting the stage for a deeper discussion of causal efficacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the objector challenges the very possibility of a non-necessary influencer.
2
Identify the objector's alternative
The objector proposes that power (qudra) might be the specifier. Ask yourself: what is the implication of this alternative for the proof of will?
The objector is not merely denying the proof but offering a different causal account.
3
Notice how the response begins
The response starts with 'We say' and begins to distinguish will from power. Observe the logical move: the response argues that will is suitable for preponderation in a way that power is not.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full argument continues beyond this excerpt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : قد بينا أن المؤثر الذي يؤثر لا على سبيل الإيجاب ولا على سبيل التكوين محال في العقول ، فلا فائدة في الإعادة . فما ذكرناه برهان قاطع في مقابلة ما ذكرتم . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك التخصيص هو القدرة ؟ قوله : لأن القدرة نسبتها إلى الإيجاد في جميع الأوقات على السوية . قلنا : إما أن نقول : الإرادة صالحة لترجيح هذا الفعل بوقت آخر ، كما أنها صالحة لترجيحه بهذا الوقت. وإما أن يقولوا : إنها صالحة لتخصيص العالم بذلك الوقت ، مع أنه يمتنع تعلقها بتخصيص
An objector may say: We have shown that the influencer that influences not by way of necessitation nor by way of origination is intellectually impossible. So there is no benefit in repetition. What we have mentioned is a decisive proof (burhan qati') in opposition to what you mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the influencer in that specification be power (qudra)? He said: Because power's relation to existence in all times is equal. We say: Either we say that will is suitable for preponderating this act at another time, just as it is suitable for preponderating it at this time. Or they say that it is suitable for specifying the world to that time, while it is impossible for it to be related to specifying...
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an objection (i'tirad) to the previous proof and the beginning of a response. The objector raises a fundamental challenge: the proof claims the specifier influences without necessitation or origination, but the objector argues such an influencer is rationally impossible. He then offers an alternative: perhaps power (qudra) can be the specifier, despite the earlier argument. The objector anticipates the reply that power's relation to all times is equal, but he challenges this by saying that will itself would also have equal relation to different times (since it could preponderate the act at another time). The response (beginning with 'We say') starts to address this by distinguishing that will is specifically suited for preponderation. However, the text ends abruptly, suggesting the response continues beyond the available page. This objection highlights a key point of contention: whether a non-necessary influencer is conceivable or not.

Why Knowledge Alone Cannot Explain Divine Action
Author refutes two interpretations of knowledge causing divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge alone cannot explain God's actions, and why another attribute (will) is necessary.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows action; attributing action to knowledge leads to circular reasoning.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle but crucial point: does God's knowledge of benefit or harm cause Him to act? He examines two interpretations and shows both fail. This discussion clarifies that God's will, not His knowledge, is the direct cause of His actions, preserving divine freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the author presents two interpretations and then refutes each.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find where the author introduces the first interpretation (knowledge of benefit calls to act) and the second (knowledge of benefit/harm directly causes action).
3
Understand each refutation
The first is refuted because it implies God acts for purposes (impermissible). The second is refuted because it leads to circularity (knowledge depends on occurrence, which depends on specification).
Circularity (دور) is a logical fallacy.
4
Note the conclusion
The author concludes that another attribute—will—must be the source of specification, not knowledge or power.
This attribute is distinct from life, hearing, sight, and speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن علمه بما في الفعل من المصلحة يدعوه إليه، أو المراد أن علمه بأن الشيء الفلاني نفع يدعوه إلى الفعل، وأن الشيء الفلاني لا نفع فيه يدعوه إلى الترك. والأول باطل، لأن كل دليل دلّ على أنه لا يجوز تعليل أفعال الله تعالى بالعلل والأغراض، فإنه يدل على بطلان هذا القسم. وأما القسم الثاني فهو أيضاً باطل. لأن العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع، الذي هو تبع لهذا التخصيص، فلو عللنا هذا التخصيص بالعلم بالوقوع لزم الدور وأنه محال، فيثبت أن هذا التخصيص غير واقع بسبب القدرة، ولا بسبب العلم، فلا بد من صفة أخرى مقتضية لهذا التخصيص والترجيح. وظاهر أن الحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام لا يصلح لذلك. فلا بد من صفة أخرى سوى هذه الصفات.
That His knowledge of the benefit in the act calls Him to it, or that His knowledge that a certain thing is beneficial calls Him to the act, and that a certain thing has no benefit in it calls Him to abandonment. The first is false, because every proof that indicates that it is not permissible to attribute the actions of God Almighty to causes and purposes indicates the falsity of this division. As for the second division, it too is false. Because knowledge of occurrence is consequent upon occurrence, which is consequent upon this specification. So if we attributed this specification to knowledge of occurrence, circularity would follow, and that is impossible. Thus it is established that this specification does not occur due to power, nor due to knowledge, so there must be another attribute that necessitates this specification and preponderance. It is apparent that life, hearing, sight, and speech are not suitable for that. So there must be another attribute besides these attributes.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that divine knowledge alone can cause action. The author examines two possible interpretations of how knowledge might lead to action. The first interpretation is that God's knowledge of benefit in an act calls Him to perform it. This is rejected because it implies that God acts for purposes, which is not permissible. The second interpretation is that God's knowledge that something is beneficial calls Him to do it, and knowledge of harm calls Him to avoid it. This is also false because knowledge of an event's occurrence depends on the event itself, which in turn depends on God's specification. Attributing specification to knowledge would create a circular dependence. Therefore, specification cannot come from power or knowledge; another attribute (will) is needed. The other attributes (life, hearing, sight, speech) are not fit for this role, so a distinct attribute of will is necessary.

Key Terms
العلم
التخصيص
الدور
العلل والأغراض
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's knowledge of what is good directly necessitates His actions, without any other attribute.
Correction: Knowledge alone cannot necessitate action because it would imply dependence and circularity. A separate attribute of will is needed.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the second interpretation (knowledge of benefit directly causing action)?
A) Because knowledge is eternal and cannot change.
B) Because it leads to circular reasoning.
C) Because benefit is not known to God.
D) Because it implies God has purposes.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge alone cannot be the cause of action; a distinct attribute of will is necessary to avoid circularity and preserve divine independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How often do you let your knowledge remain theoretical, without a decisive will to act? What is one area where you need to move from knowing to willing today?
?
The author refutes the idea that God acts for reasons like benefit or purpose. But you often justify your actions by the benefit you gain. How does this shift your understanding of pure worship? Can you perform an action today solely out of obedience, without expecting any personal benefit?
?
If knowledge follows action, then your knowledge of your own faults may never change until you actually change your actions. What is one specific change you have been putting off, allowing your knowledge to be a substitute for transformation?

The Will's Influence as Preference and the Objection of Regress
Author explains the will's influence as preference, not necessity, and answers an objection about regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine will influences by way of preference (tarjih), not necessity or creation, and why the objection of infinite regress is invalid.

Core Idea
The will's influence is by preference, not compulsion – a key to divine free choice.

In this passage, the author explains that the will's influence in specifying possibilities is a matter of preponderance (tarjih), not necessity or creation. He then addresses an objection that any influence without compulsion is rationally impossible. Through a careful analysis of two scenarios, he shows that the objection either leads to infinite regress or undermines the very concept of free will, thus preserving the doctrine of God as a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the compressed style; the argument is dense.
2
Identify the author's claim
Find where the author states that the will's influence is by way of preference, not by way of necessity or creation.
This is the core thesis.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims that influence without compulsion or creation is impossible. See how the author sets it up.
The objection is a challenge to the very possibility of free choice.
4
Follow the two-case analysis
The author considers two possibilities: either the will can preponderate at another time or it is fixed to one time. See the consequences of each.
This is the heart of the refutation.
5
Note the final argument
The author concludes that if the will can be specified to one aspect, then power would not need the will, which is unacceptable.
This ties back to preserving God as a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تأثيرها في التخصيص إن كان على سبيل الوجوب كان المؤثر موجباً بالذات، وإن كان على سبيل الصفة فهو المطلوب، لأن هذه الصفة صفة تقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا على سبيل الإيجاب، ولا على سبيل التكوين. وذلك هو المطلوب. وهذا تقرير هذا الدليل على أحسن الوجوه. ولقائل أن يقول: قد بينا أن المؤثر الذي يؤثر لا على سبيل الإيجاب ولا على سبيل التكوين محال في العقول، فلا فائدة في الإعادة. فما ذكرناه برهان قاطع في مقابلة ما ذكرتم. ثم نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك التخصيص هو القدرة؟ قوله: لأن القدرة نسبتها إلى الإيجاد في جميع الأوقات على السوية. قلنا: إما أن نقول: الإرادة صالحة لترجيح هذا الفعل بوقت آخر، كما أنها صالحة لترجيحه بهذا الوقت. وإما أن يقولوا: إنها صالحة لتخصيص العالم بذلك الوقت، مع أنه يمتنع تعلقها بتخصيص العالم بوقت آخر. فإن كان الأول فنقول: صلاحية الصفة للوجوه الكثيرة إما أن تحوج إلى المرجح أو لا تحوج، فإن أحوجته إليه لزم افتقار الإرادة إلى مرجح آخر، وإن لم تحوجه إليه وجب أن لا تحتاج القدرة إلى الإرادة. وأما القسم الثاني: فنقول: لما كانت الإرادة أزلية ممتنعة العدم، وكانت متعلقة بأحداث العالم في وقت معين، وما كانت صالحة للتعلق بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر، فحينئذ يصير الإله موجباً بالذات غير فاعل على سبيل الصحة. ومعلوم أن هذه المسألة فرع على إثبات القادر المختار، وكل فرع يوجب فساد الأصل كان باطلاً. وأيضاً: فإذا جاز أن يقال: تلك الإرادة المخصوصة متعينة التعلق بذلك الوجه الواحد، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال تلك الإرادة المخصوصة متعينة التعلق بوجه واحد، وحينئذ تكون القدرة غنية عن الإرادة المرجحة؟ وهذا تمام البحث في هذا الدليل.
Then its influence in specification: if it is by way of necessity, the agent would be an agent by essence; but if it is by way of attribute, then it is what is sought. Because this attribute is an attribute that necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other, not by way of compulsion, nor by way of creation. That is what is sought. And this is the exposition of this proof in the best manner. And an objector may say: We have already shown that an agent that influences neither by way of compulsion nor by way of creation is impossible in the intellect, so there is no benefit in repetition. So what we have mentioned is a decisive proof in opposition to what you mentioned. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the agent in that specification be power? He says: Because the relation of power to bringing into existence in all times is uniform. We say: Either we say: the will is capable of preponderating this act at another time, just as it is capable of preponderating it at this time. Or they say: It is capable of specifying the world at that time, while it is impossible for it to be attached to specifying the world at another time. If it is the first, then we say: The capacity of the attribute for many aspects either necessitates a preponderator or does not. If it necessitates it, then the will would require another preponderator; and if it does not necessitate it, then it would be necessary that power does not need the will. As for the second division, we say: Since the will is eternal, impossible of non-existence, and is attached to the events of the world at a specific time, and it is not capable of being attached to bringing about the world at another time, then in that case the deity becomes an agent by essence, not an agent in the valid sense. And it is known that this issue is a branch of establishing the free agent, and any branch that entails the corruption of the root is invalid. Also: If it is allowed to say: that particular will is specified for attachment to that one aspect, then why is it not allowed to say: that particular will is specified for attachment to one aspect, and then power would be independent of the preponderating will? This is the conclusion of the discussion in this proof.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the mode of influence of the divine will (irada). The author first clarifies that the attribute's influence is by way of preference (tarjih), not by necessity (wujub) or creation (takwin). This is what they aim to prove. Then an objection is raised: the opponent claims that an influence that is neither by compulsion nor creation is rationally impossible. The author responds by examining two possible cases. The first case: if the will can favor an act at another time as well, then the will's capacity for many aspects either requires a separate preponderator (leading to infinite regress) or does not—if not, then power would not need will at all. The second case: if the will is necessarily tied to one specific time, then God becomes a necessary agent, not a free chooser, which undermines the very premise of free will. The author argues that this line of reasoning would corrupt the foundational belief in God's free choice. Finally, they point out that if the opponent allows the will to be specified to one aspect, they should also allow power to be independent of will. Thus the argument holds.

التخصيص
الترجيح
الوجوب
التكوين
القدرة
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any influence that is not by compulsion or creation is rationally impossible.
Correction: Influence by preference (tarjīḥ) is possible and necessary to avoid determinism, as the author demonstrates by showing the consequences of the objection.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that an influence without compulsion or creation is impossible?
A) He accepts it and redefines the will.
B) He argues that the will's influence is still possible through preference and refutes the objection by analyzing its consequences.
C) He says the objection is irrelevant.
D) He concedes that the proof fails.

Key Takeaway
The divine will influences by preference, and the objection of impossibility is refuted by showing it either leads to regress or denies free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector thought that any influence not by compulsion or creation is impossible. In your life, have you ever assumed that God must act in a certain way based on your own logic? How does this lesson call you to surrender to the reality of God's free choice, even when it defies human reasoning?
?
The author argues that if the will could only attach to one time, God would become a necessary agent. Do you treat God as a necessary cause—expecting specific outcomes based on your actions—or as a free agent who chooses? What would change in your worship if you truly believed in God's unrestrained free will?
?
The dilemma in this lesson exposes the fallacy of an infinite regress of reasons. Consider your own choices: do you act as though your decisions are determined by an endless chain of causes, or do you recognize a moment of genuine preference? How does affirming God's free will by preference challenge you to exercise your own free will in obedience to Him?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Three Arguments of the Deniers of Divine Will
Author presents three arguments against divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main philosophical arguments used by those who deny the divine attribute of will, as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Opponents of divine will argue it leads to necessity, implies human traits, or forces a false dilemma.

In this passage, the author presents three distinct arguments (hujaj) that critics of the divine attribute of will have historically used. These arguments challenge the coherence of affirming will for God by pointing out logical consequences and conceptual limitations. As you read, note how each argument targets a different aspect of the concept of will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on the structure: the author enumerates three proofs with connecting phrases.
2
Identify the first argument (perfection dilemma)
The first argument uses a conditional: if will is perfect, act is necessary; if imperfect, act is impossible. Understand why both horns are problematic.
3
Identify the second argument (conceptual limitation)
The second argument claims we only know will as inclination to benefit or avoid harm, which cannot apply to God. Any other meaning is unintelligible.
4
Identify the third argument (eternal/temporal dichotomy)
The third argument presents a dichotomy: will must be either eternal or created. Both options are deemed impossible. Think about why each is rejected.
5
Reflect on the author's stance
Note that the author does not refute these arguments here; he simply presents them. This sets up the next discussion. Consider what a response might look like.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة: واحتج المنكرون لهذه الصفة بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إنها إن كانت تامة في جميع جهات المؤثرية وجب الفعل، وإن كانت غير تامة امتنع الفعل، فالقول بإثبات هذه الإرادة محال. الحجة الثانية: إنا لا نعقل من الإرادة إلا الميل إلى جلب النفع أو إلى دفع الضرر، وذلك في حق الله محال. وأما الإرادة بمعنى آخر فلا نعقله البتة، فكان القول بإثباته لله محالاً. الحجة الثالثة: لو حصلت الإرادة لكانت إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة، والقسمان باطلان، فالقول بإثبات الإرادة باطل. وهو معلوم.
The third issue: And the deniers of this attribute argued with proofs: The first argument: If it (the will) is complete in all aspects of effective agency, the act is necessary; and if it is incomplete, the act is impossible. So the claim to affirm this will is impossible. The second argument: We do not conceive of will except as inclination to bring benefit or repel harm, and that is impossible with respect to God. As for will in another sense, we do not conceive it at all. So the claim to affirm it for God is impossible. The third argument: If will existed, it would either be eternal or temporal, and both divisions are false. So the claim to affirm will is false. And it is known.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (hujaj) used by those who deny the attribute of divine will (irada). The first argument is a dilemma: if the will is perfect in every aspect of causality, then the act must necessarily follow, making it compulsory rather than free; if it is imperfect, the act cannot occur at all. Either way, affirming will is problematic. The second argument is that human understanding of will is only as a desire for benefit or avoidance of harm, both of which are impossible for God. Any other meaning of will is unintelligible. The third argument is a temporal/eternal dichotomy: the will must be either eternal or created; both are deemed impossible: an eternal will would necessitate the eternal existence of its object, and a created will would imply change in God. The author ends by noting that these arguments are well-known, but he does not refute them in this passage (likely awaiting subsequent discussion). This lesson simply records the objections of the opponents.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المؤثرية
الميل
النفع
الضرر
القديم
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments against divine will are conclusive and irrefutable.
Correction: The author presents them as objections to be refuted later, not as final conclusions.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against divine will presented in this passage?
A) Will requires a purpose, which is impossible for God.
B) If will is perfect, the act is necessary; if imperfect, the act is impossible.
C) Will cannot be both eternal and created.
D) Will is unknown to humans.

Key Takeaway
The deniers of divine will present three distinct arguments: the perfection dilemma, the conceptual limitation, and the eternal/temporal dichotomy, all of which must be addressed in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though God's will is perfectly effective in every detail of your life, or do you find yourself acting as if some things are outside His control? What would change if you fully internalized that His will is neither deficient nor compelled?
?
When you face a trial, do you catch yourself assuming that God would only will something beneficial or harmful based on your human perspective? How can you remind yourself that His will is beyond your conception of benefit and harm, yet always wise?
?
The third argument presents a false choice: God's will must be either eternal or temporal. In your dua, do you sometimes pray as if God's will is temporal and subject to change? How can you affirm His eternal will while still pleading for His mercy?
